The Dialectical Argument from Consensus
The author presents and answers an objection equating need for a cause with need for time and place.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author uses the alleged consensus on time and place to argue for the necessity of a cause, exposing inconsistency in the opponent's position.

Core Idea
If consensus on time and place is accepted, the same reasoning demands a cause.

This passage presents a clever dialectical argument. The opponent accepts that events need a specific time and place based on the consensus of rational people. The author turns that consensus against them, arguing that the very same consensus equally supports the need for a cause. By showing that the opponent's own principle leads to the desired conclusion, the author forces a logical choice: accept both or reject both, but cannot consistently accept one and reject the other.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
First, read the objection as presented by the opponent: rational people agree that an event occurs only in a specific time and place.
Notice that the opponent is using consensus as a premise.
2
Identify the theologians' claim
The theologians accept that events need time and place, but they deny that they need a cause in the same way. The author highlights their position.
3
Understand the author's logical move
The author argues that the consensus for time/place is not stronger than the consensus for a cause. If you accept one consensus, you must accept the other, or reject both consistently.
This is a classic argument from parity.
4
Grasp the conclusion
The conclusion is that the differentiation is invalid. The opponent cannot hold that events need time/place but not a cause without being inconsistent.
The author does not necessarily believe time/place are required; he is using the opponent's own premises.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والثاني : إنا نرى العقلاء مطبقين على أن الشيء لا يحدث إلا في زمان معين [ ومكان معين ] ثم إن المتكلمين زعموا أن حدوث الشيء يتوقف على سبق زمان معين ومكان معين، وإلا لزم القول بقدم الزمان والمكان ، وإذا ثبت هذا فنقول : إطباق العقلاء على افتقار الحادث [ إلى الفاعل ليس أقوى عند العقل الأول من إطباقهم على افتقار الحادث ] إلى الزمان والمكان، فإن جاز التكذيب في أحد البابين جاز في الباب الثاني ، وإن وجب التصديق في أحد البابين ، وجب في الباب [ الثاني ] فثبت أن الفرق باطل .
And the second: We see rational people agreeing that a thing occurs only in a specific time [and specific place]. Then the theologians claimed that the occurrence of a thing depends on the preceding of a specific time and specific place, otherwise it would necessitate saying that time and place are eternal. When this is established, we say: The agreement of rational people on the need of the originated event [for an agent] is not stronger in the primary intellect than their agreement on the need of the originated event for time and place. For if it is permissible to deny in one of the two cases, it is permissible in the second; and if affirmation is necessary in one case, it is necessary in the second. Thus it is established that the differentiation is invalid.
Source pages p. 79

Commentary
This passage presents a second objection and its answer. The objection states that rational people agree that an event occurs only in a specific time and place, just as they agree that it needs a cause. The theologians accept the need for time and place but reject the idea that the need for a cause is parallel. The author argues that the consensus for both is equally strong in the primary intellect. Therefore, if one rejects the need for time and place, they must also reject the need for a cause, or accept both. Since the theologians accept the need for time and place, they should also accept the parallel argument for a cause. Thus, any attempt to differentiate between the two cases is invalid.

Key Terms
زمان (zamān, time)
مكان (makān, place)
حادث (ḥādith, originated event)
فاعل (fāʿil, agent)
قدم (qidam, eternity)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The author himself believes that time and place are necessary for an event.
Correction: The author is using the opponent's own premise dialectically; he does not necessarily endorse the necessity of time and place, but shows that the opponent's reasoning leads to an unwanted conclusion.

Checkpoint
What is the author's conclusion in the second objection?
A) Time and place are necessary for an event, but a cause is not.
B) The theologians' view that events need time and place but not a cause is inconsistent.
C) The consensus for time and place is weaker than for a cause.
D) The primary intellect differentiates between the need for a cause and the need for time and place.

Key Takeaway
The author shows that if consensus is a valid basis, it equally supports the need for a cause, so the opponent cannot consistently differentiate between the two cases.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You rely on time and place for every decision and action in your daily life. If someone denied that you need a specific time to do something, you would find it absurd. Yet do you deny the need for a cause for your own existence? Why do you accept one consensus and ignore the other? What would it take for you to be consistent?
?
The theologians accepted the necessity of time and place for events, but rejected the necessity of a Creator. Examine your own heart: are there areas of your life where you apply cause and effect rigorously, but refuse to apply it to your own dependence on Allah? Will you repent from this inconsistency?
?
Consensus of rational people is what the opponent relies on. Do you truly believe that the cumulative wisdom of humanity points to anything? If so, why do you resist the clear conclusion that the universe requires a Maker? What excuse will you offer when you stand before Him having ignored the very logic you use every day?

Third Argument: Unreliability of Common Judgment
Shows theologians' own practice contradicts reliance on common agreement.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author refutes the argument from common agreement by exposing an inconsistency in the theologians' own reasoning.

Core Idea
Common agreement is not a reliable basis for causality because theologians reject it in parallel examples.

In this third argument, the author challenges those who rely on the common judgment that every event needs a cause. He presents a concrete example involving a building to reveal an inconsistency in their own reasoning. By examining how theologians treat this example, we see that they do not consistently accept common agreement, which undermines their reliance on it for causality.

Guided Path
1
Read and visualize the building example
Read the passage carefully and imagine the scenario: a building appearing in a desert without any human builder. Notice how people would react.
The author uses a vivid example to highlight common intuition.
2
Identify the common judgment about the building
Identify that rational people judge that a building cannot appear without a human builder. This is the common judgment being appealed to.
3
See how theologians allow alternative causes
Notice that theologians allow God, angels, or jinn to create the building without a human. Thus, they do not accept the common judgment in this case.
This is the key inconsistency.
4
Understand the inconsistency revealed
Reflect on the contradiction: theologians rely on common judgment for causality but reject it for the building example. This shows that common judgment is not infallible.
5
Apply the conclusion to the causality argument
Understand that if common judgment can be wrong here, then the premise 'a contingent event must have a cause'—which relies on common judgment—is also weakened.
The author is not denying causality, only the method of proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والثالث : وهو أن العقلاء يجزمون بأن البناء المعين في الدار والمدرسة لا يحصل إلا من إنسان يتولى إصلاح حال ذلك البناء فأما أن يحدث ذلك البناء من غير أن يتولاه إنسان ، فذلك مستبعد عند الكل ، حتى إن إنساناً من لو ادعى أنه غاب بالأمس عن الصحراء المعينة ، ثم عاد إليها في اليوم الثاني فوجد الدور والبساتين قد حدثت من غير أن يحضر هناك إنسان يتولى إصلاحها ، فإن جمهور العقلاء يكذبون هذا القول ، فهذا الجزم حاصل في عقول الجمهور ، ثم إن هذا الجزم باطل عند المتكلمين فإنهم يجوزون أن يخلق الله تعالى ذلك البناء ابتداء من غير واسطة إنسان ، ويجوزون أيضاً أن ملكاً الملائكة تولى إصلاحه ، أو واحداً من الجن والشياطين تولى إصلاحه ، فثبت بهذا : أن المتكلمين أطبقوا على أنه ليس كل ما يحكم به جمهور الخلق بحسب ما لهم من الفطرة الأصلية والغريزة السليمة ، وجب أن يكون ذلك الحكم حقاً ، وإذا ثبت هذا وثبت أنه لا تعويل لكم على صحة قولكم : « إن الحادث لا بد له من محدث » إلا على أن جمهور الخلق يحكمون بصحة هذه المقدمة بمقتضى الفطرة الأصلية ، ثبت أن هذه المقدمة ضعيفة .
And the third: It is that rational people affirm that a specific building in a house and school does not occur except from a human who undertakes the construction of that building. As for that building occurring without a human undertaking it, that is deemed improbable by everyone, such that if a man claimed that he was absent yesterday from a specific wilderness and then returned on the second day and found houses and gardens had occurred without a human present to undertake their construction, the majority of rational people would deny this statement. So this affirmation is present in the minds of the majority. Then this affirmation is invalid according to the theologians, for they allow that God Almighty creates that building directly without the mediation of a human, and they also allow that a king of the angels undertook its construction, or one of the jinn or devils undertook its construction. Thus it is established by this that the theologians have agreed that not everything that the majority of people judge according to their original disposition and sound instinct must necessarily be true. And when this is established and it is established that you have no reliance on the correctness of your statement: 'A contingent event must have a cause,' except that the majority of people judge the validity of this premise according to the original disposition, it is established that this premise is weak.
Source pages p. 79

Commentary
This is the third argument. The author appeals to a common example: people affirm that a specific building requires a human builder. If someone claimed that a building appeared in a desert without any human involvement, people would deny it. However, the theologians themselves allow that God could create the building directly, or an angel or jinn could build it, without a human. Thus, the theologians do not accept the common judgment that a building requires a human builder. Therefore, they cannot consistently appeal to the common judgment that every contingent event needs a cause. The premise that the majority's judgment based on original disposition is always correct is shown to be false, so the premise 'a contingent event needs a cause' is weakened.

Key Terms
العقلاء
الفطرة الأصلية
الغريزة السليمة
المتكلمين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The author is arguing that buildings do not need a builder.
Correction: He is only showing that common agreement is not an infallible guide for causality; he does not deny causality itself.

Checkpoint
Why does the author say that the theologians' position on the building example undermines their argument?
A) Because theologians deny that buildings need builders
B) Because theologians accept that common judgments are always true
C) Because theologians allow exceptions to common judgments, showing that common agreement is not infallible
D) Because the building example is irrelevant

Key Takeaway
Theologians cannot rely on common agreement for causality when they themselves reject common agreement in analogous cases.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a belief you have held as 'obvious' or 'common sense'—perhaps about how the world works. When was the last time you truly considered that God could have brought it about in a different way, without the means you assumed? Will you actively remind yourself of that divine power the next time that belief arises?
?
This lesson shows that even rational people can be mistaken when they follow their natural disposition without divine guidance. What is one area of your life where your 'gut feeling' might be contradicting what Allah has revealed? How will you test that feeling against the Qur'an and Sunnah today?
?
The theologians in the passage allowed for angels or jinn to build a house—what does that imply about your own confidence in the 'normal' causes you see? If everything is ultimately from Allah, how should that change your reaction when a plan fails or succeeds through human means? What step can you take this week to attribute outcomes more directly to Him?

The First Aspect: No Acquired Conceptualizations
Denial of the possibility of acquired conceptualizations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first aspect of the answer to the third objection: that no conceptualization can be acquired, and why this refutes the objection.

Core Idea
Acquired conceptualizations are impossible—this is established in logic.

In responding to the objection that our conceptualizations are acquired and thus not self-evident, the author first denies that any conceptualization can be acquired at all. This is a foundational principle from logic, and it directly undercuts the objection's premise. Let's examine this claim and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage for the first aspect and its translation. Notice that the author states a denial and then references a logical principle.
The phrase 'قد مر تقرير هذا الأصل' indicates the principle was explained earlier.
2
Understand the claim
The author says: 'We do not concede that any conceptualization (taṣawwur) can be acquired (kasbī).' Acquired means gained through reasoning, not innate. The objection assumes conceptualizations are acquired, so if they cannot be, the objection fails.
3
Connect to the logical principle
The author refers to the beginning of logic where this principle was established. This means the reader is expected to accept it as settled. Think about why conceptualizations must be innate or intuitive.
This is a key move: appealing to prior established knowledge.
4
Reflect on the effect on the objection
If no conceptualization is acquired, then the objection that the judgments are weak because their concepts are acquired collapses. The author has given a strong first response.
This is a direct refutation of the objection's basis.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الجواب عن السؤال الثالث : فمن وجهين : الأول : إنا لا نسلم أن شيئاً من التصورات ، يمكن أن يكون كسبياً . وقد مر تقرير هذا الأصل ، في أول علم المنطق . والثاني : سلمنا أن التصور يمكن أن يكون كسبياً نستحضر ماهية الممكن ، وماهية الاحتياج، وماهية المؤثر على أقصى الوجوه في عقولنا وأذهاننا ، فإن الممكن ، لا تفسير له ، إلا الذي يقبل الوجود والعدم ، من حيث هو هو. أو أنه الذي لا يلزم من فرض وجوده وفرض عدمه ، من حيث هو هو محال . وأما الافتقار ، فلا تفسير له ، إلا أنه هو الاحتياج والتوقف وأما المؤثر فلا تفسير له ، إلا الأمر الذي به ، ولأجله يحصل ذلك الشيء . إذا عرفت هذا فنقول : إنا بعد استحضارنا هذه التصورات الثلاثة في عقولنا على أقصى الوجوه . إذا نظرنا إلى جزم العقل بصحة قولنا : الممكن لا أحد طرفيه على الآخر إلا لمرجح ، ثم نظرنا إلى جزم العقل بأن الواحد نصف الاثنين، علمنا بالضرورة أن الجزم الأول أضعف من الجزم الثاني بكثير ، وهذا التفاوت ليس لأجل التفاوت في التصورات ، فأما في هذه الحالة فقد استحضرنا هذه التصورات على أبلغ الوجوه ، فعلمنا أن هذا التفاوت إنما وقع في الحكم والتصديق .
As for the answer to the third question—it is from two aspects: First: We do not concede that any conceptualization (taṣawwur) can be acquired (kasbī). This principle has already been established at the beginning of the science of logic. Second: Even if we concede that conceptualization can be acquired, we bring to mind the essence (māhiyya) of the possible (mumkin), the essence of need (iḥtiyāj), and the essence of the effective cause (muʾaththir) in the most complete way in our minds and intellects. For the possible has no explanation except that which accepts existence and nonexistence, in itself; or that which does not entail from assuming its existence and its nonexistence, in itself, an impossibility. As for dependence (iftiqār), it has no explanation except that it is need and reliance. As for the effective cause, it has no explanation except that thing by which and for which that thing obtains. Once you know this, we say: After we bring these three conceptualizations to mind in the most complete way, when we look at the intellect's certainty (jazm) about the truth of our statement 'the possible does not have one of its two sides preponderating over the other except by a determinant (murjīḥ),' and then we look at the intellect's certainty that one is half of two, we know by necessity that the first certainty is much weaker than the second certainty. And this disparity is not due to disparity in the conceptualizations, for in this case we have brought these conceptualizations to mind in the most complete way. So we know that this disparity has only occurred in the judgment (ḥukm) and assent (taṣdīq).
Source pages p. 82

Commentary
This passage answers the third objection to the proof for the need of a determinant. The objector likely claimed that the concepts involved (possible, need, cause) are acquired, so the judgment based on them is not self-evident. The author gives two responses. First: He denies that any conceptualization can be acquired (kasbī), referencing a principle from logic. Second: He concedes for argument that they are acquired, then shows that even when we bring these concepts (māhiyya of the possible, need, and effective cause) to mind in the most perfect way, the resulting certainty that the possible needs a determinant is still weaker than the certainty that one is half of two. This disparity is not due to the concepts themselves, since they are equally vivid; rather, it lies in the judgment itself. Thus the objection that weakness stems from acquired concepts is refuted.

Key Terms
تصور
كسبي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that conceptualizations like 'possible' are acquired through learning and reasoning, so they are not self-evident.
Correction: The author denies that any conceptualization can be acquired at all; they are innate or intuitive, as established in logic.

Checkpoint
What does the author assert in the first aspect of the answer?
A) Conceptualizations can be acquired but are still self-evident.
B) No conceptualization can be acquired.
C) Only some conceptualizations are acquired.
D) The objection is correct.

Key Takeaway
The first aspect denies the possibility of acquired conceptualizations, undermining the objection.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If all conceptualizations are either innate or intuitive and not acquired through reasoning, what does that mean for your certainty about the existence of God? Is your concept of God something you arrived at through argument, or is it already within you? What would happen if you lost all arguments—would your concept of God remain?
?
The author says the principle that no conceptualization is acquired is established in logic. But do you personally rely on logical proofs to affirm your beliefs, or do you find that your deepest convictions are not derived from reasoning? Reflect on which type of knowledge—evidential or intuitive—is more certain for you. Which are you relying on right now?

The Second Aspect: Even If Acquired, Weakness Is in Judgment
Demonstration that weakness of certainty lies in judgment, not concepts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second aspect: that even if conceptualizations were acquired, the weakness of the certainty about the possible needing a determinant lies in the judgment, not the concepts.

Core Idea
Even perfectly clear concepts yield weaker certainty for causal judgments than for mathematical ones.

The author now concedes for argument that conceptualizations can be acquired. He shows that even when we bring to mind the essences of the possible, need, and effective cause in the most perfect way, the resulting certainty that the possible needs a determinant is still weaker than the certainty that one is half of two. This disparity, he argues, must be in the judgment itself, not in the concepts. Let's follow his reasoning closely.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage for the second aspect and its translation. Note the definitions of the three concepts and the comparison of two certainties.
The passage is dense; read it slowly.
2
Identify the three conceptualizations
The author defines the essence of the possible, the essence of need, and the essence of the effective cause. Understand each definition. They are brought to mind 'in the most complete way.'
The possible is defined as that which accepts existence and non-existence, or whose existence and non-existence entail no impossibility.
3
Compare the two certainties
The author compares the certainty that the possible needs a determinant with the certainty that one is half of two. He states the first is much weaker.
We know the second is self-evident. The first is also rational but less compelling.
4
Understand the conclusion
Since the concepts are equally clear, the disparity cannot be due to the concepts. Therefore, the weakness must be in the judgment itself.
This refutes the objection that the weakness comes from acquired concepts.
5
Reflect on the argument's strength
The author uses a thought experiment: even with perfect concepts, the judgments vary in strength, proving the issue is not conceptual clarity but the nature of the judgment.
This is a powerful rebuttal.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الجواب عن السؤال الثالث : فمن وجهين : الأول : إنا لا نسلم أن شيئاً من التصورات ، يمكن أن يكون كسبياً . وقد مر تقرير هذا الأصل ، في أول علم المنطق . والثاني : سلمنا أن التصور يمكن أن يكون كسبياً نستحضر ماهية الممكن ، وماهية الاحتياج، وماهية المؤثر على أقصى الوجوه في عقولنا وأذهاننا ، فإن الممكن ، لا تفسير له ، إلا الذي يقبل الوجود والعدم ، من حيث هو هو. أو أنه الذي لا يلزم من فرض وجوده وفرض عدمه ، من حيث هو هو محال . وأما الافتقار ، فلا تفسير له ، إلا أنه هو الاحتياج والتوقف وأما المؤثر فلا تفسير له ، إلا الأمر الذي به ، ولأجله يحصل ذلك الشيء . إذا عرفت هذا فنقول : إنا بعد استحضارنا هذه التصورات الثلاثة في عقولنا على أقصى الوجوه . إذا نظرنا إلى جزم العقل بصحة قولنا : الممكن لا أحد طرفيه على الآخر إلا لمرجح ، ثم نظرنا إلى جزم العقل بأن الواحد نصف الاثنين، علمنا بالضرورة أن الجزم الأول أضعف من الجزم الثاني بكثير ، وهذا التفاوت ليس لأجل التفاوت في التصورات ، فأما في هذه الحالة فقد استحضرنا هذه التصورات على أبلغ الوجوه ، فعلمنا أن هذا التفاوت إنما وقع في الحكم والتصديق .
As for the answer to the third question—it is from two aspects: First: We do not concede that any conceptualization (taṣawwur) can be acquired (kasbī). This principle has already been established at the beginning of the science of logic. Second: Even if we concede that conceptualization can be acquired, we bring to mind the essence (māhiyya) of the possible (mumkin), the essence of need (iḥtiyāj), and the essence of the effective cause (muʾaththir) in the most complete way in our minds and intellects. For the possible has no explanation except that which accepts existence and nonexistence, in itself; or that which does not entail from assuming its existence and its nonexistence, in itself, an impossibility. As for dependence (iftiqār), it has no explanation except that it is need and reliance. As for the effective cause, it has no explanation except that thing by which and for which that thing obtains. Once you know this, we say: After we bring these three conceptualizations to mind in the most complete way, when we look at the intellect's certainty (jazm) about the truth of our statement 'the possible does not have one of its two sides preponderating over the other except by a determinant (murjīḥ),' and then we look at the intellect's certainty that one is half of two, we know by necessity that the first certainty is much weaker than the second certainty. And this disparity is not due to disparity in the conceptualizations, for in this case we have brought these conceptualizations to mind in the most complete way. So we know that this disparity has only occurred in the judgment (ḥukm) and assent (taṣdīq).
Source pages p. 82

Commentary
This passage answers the third objection to the proof for the need of a determinant. The objector likely claimed that the concepts involved (possible, need, cause) are acquired, so the judgment based on them is not self-evident. The author gives two responses. First: He denies that any conceptualization can be acquired (kasbī), referencing a principle from logic. Second: He concedes for argument that they are acquired, then shows that even when we bring these concepts (māhiyya of the possible, need, and effective cause) to mind in the most perfect way, the resulting certainty that the possible needs a determinant is still weaker than the certainty that one is half of two. This disparity is not due to the concepts themselves, since they are equally vivid; rather, it lies in the judgment itself. Thus the objection that weakness stems from acquired concepts is refuted.

The Possible's Need is Not Self-Evident
Author asserts non-self-evidence by appealing to disagreement.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author argues that the possible's need for a determinant is not a self-evident premise, and why disagreement among rational thinkers supports this.

Core Idea
Disagreement among rational thinkers shows that the premise is not self-evident.

This lesson introduces the second proof against the self-evident nature of the possible's need for a determinant. The author points to a significant historical disagreement among rational thinkers. If the premise were truly self-evident, it would not be contested. This sets up the need for a rational demonstration.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Carefully read the Arabic passage and its literal translation. Notice the terms used: 'iftiqār', 'mumkin', 'murjīḥ', 'badīhiyya'.
The passage is the opening of the second proof.
2
Identify the author's claim
What is the main claim the author makes in this passage? He states that the dependence of the possible upon the determinant is not a self-evident premise. What evidence does he provide?
The evidence is the disagreement among 'a great group of rational thinkers'.
3
Reflect on the significance of disagreement
Consider why the author uses disagreement as evidence. If a premise were truly self-evident, would it be widely rejected by intelligent people? This reasoning shows that the premise requires proof, not that it is false.
This is a common argumentative strategy in kalām.

First Method: Possibility Causes Need
First method of answering: proving that possibility (imkan) is the cause of need.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical proof that possibility (imkan) itself, not temporality (huduth), is what makes a thing need an efficient cause.

Core Idea
Possibility, not temporality, is what makes a thing need a cause.

The objection claimed that the arguments only prove that originated things need a cause, not all possible things. To refute this, the author presents a clever proof: if we consider an eternal necessary being, we see no need for a cause. Therefore, the need must arise from either temporality or possibility. He then shows that temporality cannot be the cause, leaving possibility as the sole source of need.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Pay attention to the logical structure.
Notice the conditional reasoning: 'if we assume a thing eternal and necessary...'
2
Identify the claim and the proof structure
The author first states the method, then gives a proof. The proof uses a conditional: assuming an eternal necessary being, need is impossible.
3
Follow the elimination of temporality
The author argues that temporality (huduth) cannot be the cause of need, nor a part of the cause, nor a condition. Understand why: it is a temporal attribute, not an essential characteristic of existence.
This step is crucial for the conclusion.
4
Conclude that possibility is the cause
After eliminating temporality, only possibility remains as the efficient cause of need. This proves that the need for a cause is rooted in possibility itself.
This answers the objection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والجواب : قوله في السؤال الأول : : إن الشبهات التي ذكرتموها ، إنما تدل على أن العلم بافتقار المحدث إلى المؤثر علم ضروري ولا تدل على أن العلم بافتقار الممكن إلى [ المؤثر ] المرجع ضروري . . لم نقل في الجواب عنه طريقان : الطريق الأول : أن نقول إنه لما ثبت بالوجوه المذكورة أن العلم باحتياج المحدث إلى المؤثر أمر ضروري بديهي فنقول : إنه يلزم منه كون الإمكان محوجاً إلى المؤثر ، والدليل عليه : أنا إذا فرضنا الشيء قديماً أزلياً ومع كونه كذلك، فإذا فرضناه واجب الوجود لذاته [ فههنا إذا حصل اعتقاد كونه أزليا وحصل أيضاً اعتقاد كونه واجب الوجود لذاته ] فمع حصول هذين الاعتقادين ، يستحيل منا أن نعتقد فيه كونه محتاجاً في وجوده إلى مرجح ومؤثر ، وهذا يدل على أن منشأ الحاجة إما الحدوث وإما الإمكان ، لأنا عند فرض زوال هذين المفهومين لما امتنع الحكم علينا بالحاجة ، ثبت أن المقتضي للحاجة إما مجموع هذين القيدين أو أحدهما ، وإذا ثبت هذا فنقول : الحدوث يمتنع أن يكون علة للحاجة ، أو أن يكون جزء العلة ، أو أن يكون شرط العلة ، وإذا سقط الحدوث عن درجة الاعتبار ، بقي أن المؤثر في هذه الحاجة ليس إلا الإمكان
The answer: His statement in the first question: 'The ambiguities you mentioned only indicate that knowledge of the originated's need for the efficient cause is necessary knowledge, and do not indicate that knowledge of the possible's need for the efficient cause is necessary.' We say in answer to it two methods: The first method: We say that since it has been established by the mentioned arguments that knowledge of the originated's need for the efficient cause is necessary and intuitive, we say: It follows from that that possibility is what necessitates need of the efficient cause. The proof of that: If we assume a thing to be eternal and uncreated, and if we assume it to be necessary of existence by itself, then with these two beliefs it is impossible for us to believe that it is in need of a determinant and efficient cause. This shows that the source of need is either temporality (huduth) or possibility (imkan). For when we assume the removal of these two concepts, the judgment of need becomes impossible, so it is established that the requirer of need is either the combination of these two conditions or one of them. When this is established, we say: Temporality cannot be the cause of need, nor a part of the cause, nor a condition of the cause. When temporality falls from consideration, what remains is that the efficient cause in this need is nothing but possibility.
Source pages p. 84

Commentary
This passage presents the first method of answering the objection that the arguments only prove that originated things need a cause, not that possible things as such need a cause. The author argues: once it is established that originated things need a cause (a self-evident truth), it follows that possibility (imkan) is what causes the need. The proof is: if we consider an eternal necessary being, we cannot conceive it needing a cause. Therefore the need must come from either temporality (huduth) or possibility (imkan). By analyzing, temporality cannot be the cause because it is not an essential attribute, so possibility remains as the sole cause of need. Thus the need for a cause is rooted in possibility, not just temporality.

Correction: The author proves that possibility itself necessitates a cause, even for eternal possible things, because temporality is not the cause.

Answer to second objection: matter and time
Responds to objection that originated things need matter and time.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how theologians refute the claim that originated things require pre-existing matter and time, by showing that origination from nothing is already accepted for forms and accidents.

Core Idea
Causality does not need matter; forms arise from nothing, so substances can too.

This passage tackles a key objection against creation from nothing. Critics argue that every originated thing needs matter and time, not just a cause. The author shows that this objection fails: philosophers already accept matter/time, and theologians prove that matter and time themselves are originated, so the argument is circular.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Get a feel for the author's argumentative flow.
The passage addresses two groups: philosophers and theologians.
2
Identify the two groups addressed
Notice how the objection applies differently to philosophers (who accept pre-existing matter/time) and theologians (who affirm their origination).
3
Understand the theologians' argument for matter
Focus on the reasoning: forms and accidents are accepted to arise from pure non-existence, so why cannot substances do the same? This undercuts the need for pre-existing matter.
This is the key move against the objection.
4
Understand the theologians' argument for time
Examine the time argument: parts of time precede others without requiring another time, otherwise an infinite regress would occur. This shows time does not need a pre-existing time.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما السؤال الثاني : قولهم : ( إن العقول كما جزمت بأنه لابد للحادث من فاعل ، فكذلك جزمت بأنه لابد للحادث من سبق مادة [ ذهبت ] ومدة ومكان . . فنقول : هذا السؤال غير وارد على الفلاسفة ، فإنهم يلتزمون [ أن الحادث ] كما أنه لابد له من فاعل [ سابق ] فكذلك لابد له من مادة سابقة ، ومن مدة سابقة [ وبهذا الطريق ] فهذا السؤال غير وارد عليهم . وأما القائلون بحدوث المادة والمدة ، فقالوا : الفرق بين البابين ظاهر ، أما المادة فلانه لا نزاع أن الصور والأعراض تحدث عن محض العـدم ، [ وإذا عدلنا حدوث بعض الأشياء عن محض العدم ] فكيف يمتنع في [ أول ] العقل حدوث الذوات أيضاً عن محض العدم ؟ وأما المدة فقالوا لاشك أن بعض أجزائها سابق على البعض ، لا لأجل مدة أخرى وإلا لزم وقوع المدة في مدة
As for the second question: Their statement: 'Just as minds have determined that the originated necessarily has a doer, so too they have determined that the originated necessarily requires a preceding matter, time, and place.' We say: This objection does not apply to the philosophers, for they affirm that the originated, just as it necessarily has a preceding doer, so too it necessarily has a preceding matter and a preceding time. Thus this objection does not apply to them. As for those who affirm the origination of matter and time, they said: The difference between the two cases is apparent. As for matter, there is no dispute that forms and accidents arise from pure non-existence. If we have admitted the origination of some things from pure non-existence, how can it be impossible, in the beginning of reason, that substances also arise from pure non-existence? As for time, they said: There is no doubt that some of its parts precede others, not because of another time, otherwise it would lead to a time within a time.
Source pages p. 85

Commentary
This passage answers the second objection: the claim that originated things must have preceding matter, time, and place, not just a cause. The author distinguishes: Philosophers (falasifa) already accept that originated things need matter and time, so the objection does not apply to them. Theologians (mutakallimun) who affirm the origination of matter and time argue differently: For matter, they note that forms and accidents are already accepted to arise from pure non-existence, so why can't substances? For time, they point out that some parts of time precede others without needing another time, else there would be infinite time. Thus, the need for a cause does not imply the need for pre-existing matter or time.

Correction: The passage shows that forms and accidents originate from pure non-existence, so substances can too, and time does not need a preceding time.

Key Takeaway

The Second Objection: Need for Matter, Time, and Place
Sanusi refutes the need for prior matter and time in origination.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Sanusi refutes the objection that originated things require prior matter and time, and why the intellect only necessitates a maker, not prior materials.

Core Idea
The intellect demands a maker, but not necessarily matter or time.

Sanusi tackles the second objection against Divine creation: if everything needs a cause, it also needs prior matter and time. He shows this does not hold by examining forms, accidents, and time-parts, proving the intellect only requires a maker.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then read the literal translation. Let the author's argument flow before interpreting.
Notice that the passage starts by stating the objection from the opponents.
2
Understand the objection and its scope
Identify the objection: just as the intellect necessitates a maker for originated things, it also necessitates prior matter and time. Note that this objection does not affect the philosophers.
The philosophers already accept prior matter and time, so the objection is irrelevant to them.
3
Examine the refutation regarding matter
Focus on the argument about matter: forms and accidents arise from pure non-existence, so why can't substances? This shows the intellect allows origination from nothing.
The key point is that we already accept origination from nothing in some cases.
4
Examine the refutation regarding time
Analyze the argument for time: priority of time-parts cannot be due to another time, else infinite regress. Thus, the intellect accepts priority without a time, analogously to origination.
This demonstrates that the intellect does not require a prior time for origination.
5
Synthesize the conclusion
Notice the final conclusion: the intellect requires a maker absolutely, but not matter or time. The difference is clear.
This reinforces the doctrine of creation from nothing (creatio ex nihilo).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما السؤال الثاني : قولهم : ( إن العقول كما جزمت بأنه لابد للحادث من فاعل ، فكذلك جزمت بأنه لابد للحادث من سبق مادة ذهبت ومدة ومكان . فنقول : هذا السؤال غير وارد على الفلاسفة ، فإنهم يلتزمون أن الحادث كما أنه لابد له من فاعل سابق فكذلك لابد له من مادة سابقة ، ومن مدة سابقة وبهذا الطريق فهذا السؤال غير وارد عليهم . وأما القائلون بحدوث المادة والمدة ، فقالوا : الفرق بين البابين ظاهر ، أما المادة فلانه لا نزاع أن الصور والأعراض تحدث عن محض العدم ، وإذا عدلنا حدوث بعض الأشياء عن محض العدم فكيف يمتنع في أول العقل حدوث الذوات أيضاً عن محض العدم ؟ وأما المدة فقالوا لاشك أن بعض أجزائها سابق على البعض ، لا لأجل مدة أخرى وإلا لزم وقوع المدة في مدة أخرى ، ولزم التسلسل وهو محال . فثبت أن سبق الجزء في المدة ، على الجزء المتأخر منها ليس لأجل مدة اخرى وإذا عقل هذا ، فلم لا يعقل سبق عدم الحادث على وجوده من غير مدة ؟ فثبت بهذين الطريقين: أن صريح العقل لا يقتضي افتقار الحادث في حدوثه إلى سبق مادة ومدة، لأنه حكم في هاتين الصورتين بالحدوث من غير سبق مادة ولا مدة ، أما في حق الفاعل ، فقد حكم صريح العقل بالافتقار الى الفاعل ، ولم يوجد شيء حدث ، ولا عن الفاعل فظهر الفرق .
As for the second question: their saying: 'Just as the intellects have judged that the originated necessarily has a maker, so too they have judged that the originated necessarily has a prior material that has elapsed, a time, and a place.' We say: this question does not apply to the philosophers (falasifa), for they affirm that the originated, just as it necessarily has a prior maker, so also it necessarily has a prior material and a prior time, and by this way this question does not apply to them. As for those who affirm the origination of matter and time, they said: the difference between the two issues is apparent. As for matter, because there is no dispute that forms and accidents arise from pure non-existence, and if we have admitted the origination of some things from pure non-existence, then how can it be impossible in the first [judgment] of the intellect that substances also arise from pure non-existence? As for time, they said: there is no doubt that some of its parts are prior to others not because of another time, otherwise there would be a time within a time and infinite regress, which is impossible. Thus it is established that the priority of one part of time over another part is not due to another time. And if this is understood, then why is it not understood that the non-existence of the originated is prior to its existence without a time? Thus it is established by these two ways that the clear intellect does not necessitate that the originated in its origination needs a prior matter and time, because it has judged in these two cases [forms and time-parts] that origination occurs without prior matter or time. As for the case of the maker, the clear intellect has judged the need for a maker, and nothing has occurred [except from the maker]? So the difference is clear.
Source pages p. 85

Commentary
This passage addresses the second question: an objection that the intellect also demands a prior material, time, and place for the originated, just as it demands a maker. The author first notes that this objection does not affect the philosophers, who already accept a prior matter and time. But for those who affirm the origination of matter and time themselves, they argue as follows. Regarding matter: forms and accidents arise from nothing, so why not substances? This shows the intellect does not prohibit origination from nothing. Regarding time: priority of one part over another cannot be due to another time on pain of infinite regress; thus the intellect accepts priority without a time. Hence, by analogy, the non-existence of an originated thing can be prior to its existence without a time. Therefore, the mind does not necessitate a prior matter or time for origination, but does necessitate a maker. The difference is clear: the need for a maker is absolute, while the need for matter and time is not.

فاعل (fa'il)
مادة (maddah)
مدة (muddah)
مكان (makan)
حدوث (huduth)
تسلسل (tasalsul)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that if an originated thing needs a cause, it also needs a prior material.
Correction: The intellect only necessitates a maker, not prior matter or time, as shown by forms and accidents arising from nothing and time-parts being prior without another time.

Checkpoint
According to the response, why does the intellect not necessitate a prior matter for all originated things?
A) Because forms and accidents arise from nothing, so substances can too
B) Because matter is eternal
C) Because the intellect has no judgment about matter
D) Because the philosophers reject matter

Key Takeaway
Sanusi demonstrates that the intellect's judgment for a maker is absolute, but for matter and time it is conditional; origination without prior matter or time is rationally conceivable.

Will you consciously pause today to recognize God as the sole, unconstrained originator of every moment?
?
Your intellect tells you that every event has a cause. But do you secretly believe that God also needs 'raw materials' or a proper 'time' to act? Examine your heart: is there any area where you limit God's power by imagining He cannot intervene without preconditions? How will you actively reject that limiting thought and submit to His absolute freedom today?
?
If time itself is created, then the interval between your sin and your repentance is not a fixed, independent timeline. The moment of repentance is a direct creation from God, not bound by prior time. Does this realization make you feel the urgency to repent now, without waiting for a 'better time'? Why Contingents Don't Need Prior Matter or Time
Author proves contingent does not require prior matter/time.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why pure reason only requires an agent for the occurrence of a contingent event, and not matter or time.

Core Idea
Contingent events need a cause, not a pre-existing material or time.

This lesson examines a key argument from the author that distinguishes between the need for a cause (agent) and the need for prior matter or time. The author uses two clear examples from time and non-existence to show that pure reason does not require material or temporal precedence for a contingent event. Instead, it only requires an agent. Understanding this distinction is crucial for avoiding misconceptions about causality.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own argument come through before you interpret.
Pay attention to the phrase 'two ways' (طريقين) as it signals the structure.
2
Identify the two arguments
The passage presents two rational proofs: one about the precedence of parts of time, and one about the non-existence of a contingent before its existence. Find each and notice how they both lead to the same conclusion.
3
Understand the key distinction
After establishing that matter and time are not required, the author contrasts this with the need for an agent. Ask yourself why the need for an agent remains necessary while matter and time are not.
This distinction is central to the author's position on causality.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أخرى ، ولزم التسلسل وهو محال . فثبت أن سبق الجزء (۱) في المدة ، على الجزء المتأخر منها ليس لأجل مدة اخرى وإذا عقل هذا ، فلم لا يعقل سبق عدم الحادث على وجوده من غير مدة ؟ فثبت بهذين الطريقين: أن صريح العقل لا يقتضي افتقار الحادث في حدوثه إلى سبق مادة ومدة، لأنه حكم في هاتين الصورتين بالحدوث من غير سبق مادة ولا مدة ، أما في حق الفاعل ، فقد حكم صريح العقل بالافتقار الى الفاعل ، ولم يوجد شيء حدث ، ولا عن الفاعل فظهر الفرق .
Another [point]; and infinite regress would follow, which is impossible. So it is established that the precedence of the earlier part in time over the later part is not due to another time. And if this is understood, then why should it not be understood that the precedence of non-existence of the contingent over its existence is without time? So it is established by these two ways: that pure reason does not require that the contingent in its origination should need the precedence of matter and time, because it judged in these two instances that origination [occurs] without the precedence of matter or time. As for the agent, pure reason has judged that it needs the agent, and there is no thing that has occurred [spontaneously] or from the agent, so the difference becomes clear.
Source pages p. 86

Commentary
This passage is a proof that a contingent event does not require a prior material substrate or a time interval for its occurrence. The author argues that if a part of time preceded another part, that precedence cannot be due to another time (else infinite regress). Similarly, the non-existence of a contingent before its existence does not require a time interval. Pure reason judges in these two cases that the contingent occurs without prior matter or time, but it does judge that it needs an agent. The difference between the need for a cause and the need for prior matter/time is thus made clear.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The need for a cause implies temporal or material precedence of that cause.
Correction: This passage shows that pure reason only requires an agent (efficient cause) for the contingent, not a prior material substrate or a time interval. The cause can be simultaneous and non-material.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what does pure reason require for the occurrence of a contingent (hadith)?
A) A prior material substrate
B) A prior time interval
C) An agent (fa'il)
D) Both matter and time

Key Takeaway
A contingent event requires an efficient cause (agent) but does not require a prior material substrate or a temporal interval for its occurrence.

Do you secretly rely on the passage of time to 'fix' things, rather than turning immediately to the Agent who can change your state without delay? How can you repent right now, without expecting time to erase the distance?

Answer to the Objection on Persisting Possibles
Author rebuts that the need for a cause is not self-evident for persisting possibles.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author defends the self-evidence of causality against the objection that persisting possibles do not appear to need a cause.

Core Idea
Persistence does not remove a possible's need for a cause.

The third objection claims that if causality were self-evident, everyone would recognize that a persisting possible still needs a cause. Since they don't, the principle is not self-evident. The author responds by identifying a false belief that blocks this recognition.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and response
Read the passage in Arabic and translation. Notice the structure: the objectors' claim, then the author's reply starting with 'we say'.
Pay attention to the brackets indicating variant readings.
2
Identify the false belief
Find why some people deny the need for a cause. The text says it is due to a belief that persistence makes the possible more worthy of existence.
This is a common misunderstanding.
3
Understand the author's argument
The author argues that if one conceives the persisting possible as being equal in both existence and non-existence, one is compelled to see its need for a cause. The false belief blocks this conception.
Equality (istiwā') is key.
4
Reflect on self-evidence
The author concludes that the statement about self-evidence is established because the doubt is due to a false belief, not a failure of self-evidence.
This defends the principle.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما المعارضة الثالثة (١) وهي قولهم : ( لو كانت هذه القضية بديهية ، لكان العلم بافتقار الممكن الباقي إلى المؤثر، علماً بديهياً . وليس كذلك .. فنقول : إن كل من تصور في الموجود الباقي كونه متساوياً ، اضطر إلى العلم بافتقاره إلى المؤثر [ نعم قد لا يحكم بافتقاره إلى المؤثر] (٢) لاعتقاد أنه لأجل كونه باقياً صار أولى بالوجود ، فأما إذا أزال عن قلبه هذه الشبهة ، وعلم أنه حال البقاء بقي متساوي الطرفين ، كما كان حال الحدوث ، اضطر إلى العلم بافتقاره إلى المؤثر . فهذا كله هو الكلام في تقرير (۳) قولنا : إن العلم بافتقار الممكن إلى المؤثر علم بديهي [ والله ولي الهداية والإرشاد ] (٤)
As for the third objection—which is their statement: 'If this proposition were self-evident, then the knowledge of the need of the persisting possible for the cause would be self-evident knowledge. But it is not so'—we say: Everyone who conceives in the persisting existent its being equal is compelled to know its need for the cause. [Indeed, he may not judge its need for the cause] due to his belief that because it is persisting, it has become more worthy of existence. But when he removes from his heart this doubt, and knows that in the state of persistence it remains equal in the two sides, just as in the state of origination, he is compelled to know its need for the cause. So all this is the discussion in establishing our statement: that knowledge of the need of the possible for the cause is self-evident knowledge. [And God is the Guide to guidance and right direction.]
Source pages p. 87

Commentary
This passage is the answer to the third objection, which argues that if the need for a cause were self-evident, then the need of a persisting possible (al-mumkin al-baqi) for a cause would also be self-evident—but it is not, since many do not sense it. The author replies that anyone who properly conceives the persisting existent as being equal in its two sides (existence and non-existence) is compelled to know its need for a cause. The reason some do not judge this is due to a false belief that persistence makes it more worthy of existence. Once that doubt is removed and they realize persistence does not remove the equality, they are compelled to recognize the need. The author concludes by affirming that the statement about the self-evident nature of the causal principle is established.

Correction: According to the author, the need for a cause remains because the possible remains equal in its two sides regardless of persistence.

Checkpoint

A) Because it is self-evident that it does not
B) Because they believe persistence makes it more worthy of existence
C) Because they are ignorant of logic

Key Takeaway
The need of a possible being for a cause remains self-evident even for persisting beings; any denial stems from a false belief about persistence granting worthiness.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?

Resolving the Third Objection on Self-Evidence
Responds to the objection that knowledge of need for a cause is not self-evident.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author defends the self-evidence of the need for a cause by showing that a false belief about persistence can obscure it.

Core Idea
Self-evident knowledge can be veiled by a false belief.

This passage tackles a common objection: if the need for a cause is self-evident, why isn't it obvious for persistent things? The author explains that the self-evident knowledge is present but masked by a mistaken assumption. Let's see how he resolves this.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Start by reading the Arabic passage and its English translation to get a feel for the argument. Don't worry about details yet.
Notice the structure: objection, then reply.
2
Identify the objection and core reply
The objection says that if the need for a cause were self-evident, it would be so for persistent contingents too. Find the author's initial reply: 'Everyone who conceives... is compelled.'
The key term is 'equally balanced' (متساوي الطرفين).
3
Examine the false belief
The author points out that some people fail to judge the need because they mistakenly think persistence makes the thing more deserving of existence. Identify this false belief in the text.
This is the obstacle to self-evident knowledge.
4
Understand the removal of the veil
Once the false belief is removed and the person sees the persistent thing as equally balanced, the self-evident knowledge returns. Trace this reasoning in the passage.
This shows the principle remains self-evident; only the application was blocked.
5
Summarize the defense
Write a one-sentence summary of how the author upholds the self-evidence claim despite the apparent counterexample.
The defense hinges on distinguishing the intrinsic self-evidence from subjective obstruction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما المعارضة الثالثة وهي قولهم : لو كانت هذه القضية بديهية ، لكان العلم بافتقار الممكن الباقي إلى المؤثر، علماً بديهياً . وليس كذلك .. فنقول : إن كل من تصور في الموجود الباقي كونه متساوياً ، اضطر إلى العلم بافتقاره إلى المؤثر نعم قد لا يحكم بافتقاره إلى المؤثر لاعتقاد أنه لأجل كونه باقياً صار أولى بالوجود ، فأما إذا أزال عن قلبه هذه الشبهة ، وعلم أنه حال البقاء بقي متساوي الطرفين ، كما كان حال الحدوث ، اضطر إلى العلم بافتقاره إلى المؤثر . فهذا كله هو الكلام في تقرير قولنا : إن العلم بافتقار الممكن إلى المؤثر علم بديهي والله ولي الهداية والإرشاد
As for the third objection, which is their saying: 'If this proposition were self-evident, then the knowledge that the persistent contingent needs a cause would be self-evident knowledge. But it is not so.' So we say: Everyone who conceives of the persistent existent as being equally balanced (mutasāwiyan) is compelled to the knowledge that it needs a cause. Yes, he may not judge that it needs a cause due to his belief that because it is persistent it has become more deserving of existence. But when he removes this doubt from his heart and knows that in the state of persistence it remains equally balanced (mutasāwi al-ṭarafayn) as it was in the state of origination, he is compelled to the knowledge that it needs a cause. All this is the discussion in establishing our statement: 'The knowledge that the contingent needs a cause is self-evident knowledge.' And God is the guide to guidance and right direction.
Source pages p. 87

Commentary
This passage is a response to a third objection against the claim that the knowledge that a contingent being needs a cause is self-evident. The objectors argue that if it were self-evident, then the knowledge that a *persistent* contingent (al-mumkin al-bāqī) needs a cause would also be self-evident, but it is not. The author replies by explaining that anyone who truly conceives of a persistent thing as being equally balanced (i.e., neither necessary nor impossible) is indeed compelled to know that it needs a cause. However, some people may not judge that it needs a cause because they hold a false belief that persistence itself makes the thing more deserving of existence. Once this false belief is removed, and the person realizes that the persistent thing remains equally balanced just as it was at origination, the self-evident knowledge follows. The author thus maintains that the knowledge is self-evident but sometimes obscured by a false premise.

Misconception: Some think that because a persistent contingent appears not to need a cause, the self-evident principle is false.
Correction: The need is obscured by a belief that persistence makes the thing more deserving of existence; removal of this belief restores the self-evident knowledge.

Checkpoint

A) They do not conceive of it as equally balanced.
B) They believe persistence makes it more deserving of existence.
C) They think causality applies only to origination.

Key Takeaway
The self-evidence of the need for a cause is not negated by the case of persistent contingents; it is only veiled by a false belief about persistence.

Refutation of the Claim that Preponderance Must Be from Essence
The author refutes an opponent's argument that preponderance must be due to essence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author refutes the claim that preponderance must be from the essence by exposing it as either a rejection of inference or a vicious circle.

Core Idea
The opponent's argument either abandons reason or circles back on itself.

In this passage, the author tackles a subtle but flawed argument about causation. The opponent claims that if preponderance is not due to an external cause, it must be due to the essence itself. The author dismantles this by showing the hidden assumptions lead to a dead end. Let's see how.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its English translation. Pay attention to the logical flow of the argument.
The author uses a conditional structure: 'if... then...'
2
Identify the opponent's claim
Find where the author states the opponent's reasoning: 'since preponderance was not due to a different cause, it must be due to the essence.'
3
Understand the author's two-pronged refutation
Notice how the author first points out that the opponent's premise needs proof, then shows that claiming intuitive knowledge abandons inference, while attempting a proof leads to circular reasoning.
The author uses a dilemma: either intuitive knowledge (abandonment of inference) or proof (vicious circle).
4
Recognize the vicious circle
See that if the opponent tries to prove the premise, that proof's validity depends on the very conclusion being argued, creating a loop.
This is called 'dawr' (circular reasoning).
5
Reflect on the conclusion
Understand why the author's refutation is effective and how it protects sound theological reasoning.
The opponent's argument fails on both counts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكان هذا الرجل قد ظن أن هذا الرجحان لما لم يكن بسبب أمر مغاير ، وجب أن يكون لذاته ، فحينئذ تكون ذاته مقتضية لهذا الرجحان ، وذلك يناقض قولنا : إن هذه الذات غير مقتضية للرجحان ، إلا أن هذا الكلام إنما يصح لو ثبت أن حصول الرجحان إذا لم يكن لغيره وجب أن يكون لذاته، وهذا إنما يصح لو ثبت أن حصول الرجحان لا لذاته ولا لغيره محال، فإذا ادعى العلم البديهي بهذه المقدمة، كان ذلك تركا للاستدلال وإن ادعى تقريرها بالدليل . فنقول : قد ثبت أن صحة الدليل الذي ذكرتموه موقوف على صحة هذا المطلوب، فلو وقفنا صحة هذا المطلوب على ذلك الدليل لزم الدور، وأنه باطل.
This man thought that since this preponderance was not due to a different cause, it must be due to the essence. Then the essence would be necessitating this preponderance, and that contradicts our statement: that this essence does not necessitate preponderance. However, this statement is only valid if it is established that if preponderance is not due to another, it must be due to the essence, and this is only valid if it is established that the obtaining of preponderance not due to the essence nor to another is impossible. So if he claims intuitive knowledge of this premise, that is an abandonment of inference; and if he claims to establish it by proof, we say: it has been established that the validity of the proof which you mentioned depends on the validity of this desired conclusion. So if we make the validity of this desired conclusion depend on that proof, a vicious circle follows, and that is invalid.
Source pages p. 89

Commentary
This passage refutes an opponent's argument. The opponent claimed that since preponderance (tarjih) is not due to an external cause, it must be due to the essence itself. The author points out that this reasoning only holds if it is impossible for preponderance to be due to neither essence nor another. If the opponent claims intuitive knowledge of this impossibility, he abandons rational inference; if he offers a proof, that proof's validity depends on the very claim in question, leading to a vicious circle (dawr), which is invalid. Thus the opponent's position is unsupported.

Key Terms
رجحان
ذات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Preponderance must be from the essence if not from an external cause.
Correction: This belief is unsupported because proving it either requires abandoning inference or leads to circular reasoning.

Checkpoint
What is the author's main objection to the opponent's argument that preponderance must be due to the essence?
A) The opponent confuses preponderance with existence.
B) The opponent's argument either abandons inference or leads to a vicious circle.
C) The opponent denies the principle of causality.
D) The opponent relies on intuitive knowledge without proof.

Key Takeaway
The author shows that the opponent's reasoning either rejects rational inference or leads to a vicious circle, thus it is unsound.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever held onto an argument simply because it felt intuitively true, without bothering to examine the premises? This lesson shows that such reliance on intuition can be a disguise for abandoning reason. Will you now commit to scrutinizing your own assumptions before defending them?
?
When you argue for a belief, do you check whether your reasoning leads to a circle? How might your faith be strengthened if you root out circular justifications and demand sound proofs from yourself and others?
?
The author exposes a flaw by forcing the opponent into a dilemma: either abandon reason or fall into circularity. Are there areas in your life where you have avoided this kind of honest self-examination? What would it cost you to face them today?

First Proof: The Cause of Need is Possibility
Sanusi presents the first proof eliminating origination and establishing possibility as the cause.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first proof that possibility, not origination, is the cause of a contingent being's need for an effect.

Core Idea
The contingent's need for a cause is due to its possibility, not its temporality.

This lesson examines the first proof that establishes possibility as the true cause of a contingent's need for an effect. The argument proceeds by eliminating origination as a candidate, leaving only possibility as the sufficient reason. Understanding this proof is crucial for the broader discussion of causality in kalam.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once through, then read the literal translation. Note the key terms: muhdath, mu'aththir, huduth, imkan, illah.
The passage is dense; take your time to grasp each term.
2
Identify the three logical candidates
The author lists three possible causes for the need: huduth (origination), imkan (possibility), or both together. Understand each as a distinct category.
This is an exhaustive enumeration of possibilities.
3
Analyze why huduth is eliminated
The author argues that huduth is not a complete cause, not a part of the cause, and not even a condition. Reflect on why temporality alone cannot necessitate a creator.
This elimination is the key move in the proof.
4
Conclude that imkan remains as the cause
With huduth removed, only imkan remains. Therefore, the need for an effect arises from the very possibility of the contingent's existence.
This conclusion sets the stage for further arguments.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إذا عرفت هذا فنقول : الذي يعول عليه في إثبات هذا المطلوب وجوه : الأول : أنا قد بينا أن العلم البديهي حاصل بافتقار المحدث إلى المؤثر ، وبينا أيضاً : أن علة تلك الحاجة إما الحدوث أو الإمكان أو مجموعهما، وبينا أيضاً أن الحدوث ليس علة تامة ولا شطر العلة ولا شرطاً لها ، فكان ساقطاً عن درجة الاعتبار بالكلية ، وإذا سقط الحدوث عن درجة الاعتبار لم يبق إلا الإمكان وهذا يدل على أن علة الحاجة هي الإمكان .
Since you have understood this, we say: What is relied upon in establishing this desired conclusion are aspects: the first: we have shown that intuitive knowledge is obtained of the contingent (muhdath) being in need of an effect (mu'aththir), and we also showed that the cause of that need is either origination (huduth) or possibility (imkan) or both together, and we also showed that origination is not a complete cause nor part of the cause nor a condition for it, so it falls completely from the degree of consideration. And when origination falls from the degree of consideration, nothing remains except possibility, and this indicates that the cause of the need is possibility.
Source pages p. 89

Commentary
This passage presents the first proof (al-wajh al-awwal) for the desired conclusion that the contingent requires an effect. The author begins with an established fact: intuitive knowledge tells us that the contingent (muhdath) is in need of an effect (mu'aththir). He then analyzes the cause of this need. There are three candidates: origination (huduth), possibility (imkan), or both. The author argues that origination is not a complete cause, not a part of the cause, and not even a condition; therefore it is entirely irrelevant. Once origination is eliminated, only possibility remains. Thus the need for a cause is due to possibility, not temporality.

Key Terms
محدث
مؤثر
حدوث
إمكان
علة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the temporariness of a thing is the reason it needs a cause, but here possibility is shown to be the actual cause.
Correction: The proof demonstrates that origination (huduth) is not a valid cause; rather, it is possibility (imkan) that necessitates a creator.

Checkpoint
According to the first proof, what is the cause of the contingent's need for an effect?
A) Origination (huduth)
B) Possibility (imkan)
C) Both origination and possibility
D) The contingent's essence

Key Takeaway
The first proof shows that only possibility, not origination, can be the cause of a contingent being's need for a creator.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If your need for God is rooted in your very possibility (that you could be nothing), not just your coming into existence, then are you living as if you need God only at the beginning, or do you recognize that every moment of your existence depends on Him? How would your day change if you truly felt that dependence now?
?
The proof eliminates temporal origin as the cause of need. Yet many people feel distant from God because they think He only acted at the start. Does your heart feel closer to God when you reflect on your possibility rather than your past? What keeps you from feeling that closeness today?
?
You are possible, not necessary, and that means you have no right to exist by yourself. When was the last time you looked at yourself and honestly said, 'I am nothing without Allah'? If that truth does not move you to gratitude and humility, what will?

Second Proof: Possibility Requires an External Agent
The author argues that a possible thing needs an external agent due to the nature of preponderance.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second proof for the necessity of an external agent (fa'il) for any possible thing (mumkin), based on the impossibility of a vicious circle in the subject of preponderance.

Core Idea
The possible cannot tip itself into existence; it needs an external agent.

In this lesson, we examine a key proof from Islamic philosophical theology that a contingent being cannot bring itself into existence. The argument hinges on the nature of a 'possible' thing (mumkin), which has no inherent bias toward existence or non-existence. For it to become existent, a preponderance must occur, and that preponderance must inhere in something distinct from the possible itself—otherwise we fall into a vicious circle. This something distinct is what we call the agent (fa'il).

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text once slowly. Focus on the logical flow from definition to conclusion.
Note the key term 'رجحان' (preponderance).
2
Understand the definition of 'possible'
The passage defines a possible as having equal relation to existence and non-existence. Grasp why this equality prevents existence without preponderance.
This is the starting point of the argument.
3
Follow the reasoning on preponderance
See how the author argues that preponderance is an existential attribute that must have a subject. Then follow the argument that this subject cannot be the possible itself.
Pay attention to the circularity argument.
4
Identify the conclusion
The conclusion is that the subject must be a distinct agent. Make sure you can articulate why this agent is necessary.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثانية في تقرير هذا المطلوب : أن نقول : لاشك أن الممكن هو الذي تكون نسبة الوجود إليه كنسبة العدم إليه، وما دام يبقى هذا الاستواء، فإنه يمتنع حصول الرجحان، لأن الاستواء التام يناقض حصول الرجحان . فثبت: أن دخوله في الوجود موقوف على حصول الرجحان وهذا الرجحان لما حصل بعد أن لم يكن ، كان أمراً وجودياً ثبوتياً ، والصفة الوجودية الثابتة لابد لها من موصوف موجود ، ويمتنع أن يكون الموصوف بهذه الصفة الوجودية هو وجود ذلك الشيء ، لأنا بينا أن حصول هذا الرجحان سابق على وجود الممكن سبقاً بالرتبة، لكن المحل سابق على ما يحل فيه سبقاً بالرتبة. فلو قلنا : إن محل هذا الرجحان هو أن تلك الذات حال صيرورتها موجودة : لزم كون هذا الرجحان سابقاً على ذلك الرجحان من حيث أن المحل سابق على الحال . وذلك دور والدور باطل ، فثبت أنه ليس محل هذا الرجحان هو ذلك الممكن الذي هو الأثر ، فلا بد من شيء آخر يغايره ، ويكون محلا لهذا الرجحان ويكون بحيث يلزم من حصول الرجحان فيه حصول الوجود لهذا الأثر ، ولا نريد بالفاعل والمؤثر إلا الموجود الذي من شأنه وصفته ما ذكرناه . فثبت أن الممكن لا يمكن دخوله في الوجود إلا لأجل مؤثر منفصل وذلك هو المطلوب
The second proof in establishing this desired conclusion: we say: There is no doubt that the possible (mumkin) is that whose relation to existence is like its relation to non-existence, and as long as this equality remains, obtaining preponderance is impossible, because complete equality contradicts obtaining preponderance. So it is established that its entry into existence depends on obtaining preponderance, and this preponderance, since it occurred after not having been, was an existential positive attribute, and an existential established attribute must have an existent subject, and it is impossible that the subject of this existential attribute is the existence of that thing, because we have shown that this preponderance is prior to the existence of the possible in rank, but the subject is prior to what inheres in it in rank. So if we say that the subject of this preponderance is that essence when it becomes existent, then this preponderance would be prior to that preponderance, insofar as the subject is prior to the attribute. That is a vicious circle, and the circle is invalid. So it is established that the subject of this preponderance is not that possible which is the effect, so there must be something else distinct from it, which is a subject for this preponderance, and is such that the obtaining of preponderance in it necessitates the obtaining of existence for this effect, and we mean by agent (fa'il) and effect (mu'aththir) nothing but the existent whose property and attribute are what we mentioned. So it is established that the possible cannot enter into existence except through a distinct agent, and that is the desired conclusion.
Source pages p. 89

Commentary
This passage presents the second proof (al-hujja al-thaniya) that a possible thing requires an external agent. It begins with the definition of a possible (mumkin): it has an equal relation to existence and non-existence. As long as this equality holds, preponderance (tarjih) of one side is impossible. Therefore, for the possible to exist, preponderance must occur. This preponderance, being a positive existential attribute, must inhere in an existent subject. The subject cannot be the existence of the possible itself because preponderance is prior to that existence in rank, and the subject is prior to its attribute. If the subject were the possible at the moment it becomes existent, a circular priority would result. Hence, the subject of preponderance must be something distinct from the possible—that which we call the agent (fa'il) or effect (mu'aththir). Thus, the possible cannot exist without a distinct external agent.

Key Terms
ممكن
وجود
عدم
استواء
رجحان
صفة وجودية
محل
فاعل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the attribute of preponderance could inhere in the effect itself (the possible thing).
Correction: The author shows this leads to a vicious circle: the preponderance would be prior to the existence of the possible, but if the possible at the moment of existence is the subject, then preponderance would be prior to itself. Hence the subject must be something else.

Checkpoint
What is the subject of the preponderance that allows a possible thing to exist?
A) The existence of the possible itself
B) The essence of the possible at the moment it becomes existent
C) A distinct agent external to the possible
D) The attribute of existence

Key Takeaway
A possible thing cannot exist without a distinct external agent because the preponderance required for its existence cannot inhere in itself without circularity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If your existence is entirely dependent on Allah's preponderance at every moment, why do you still act as if you are self-sufficient? What would change if you truly internalized this fact?
?
The proof shows that preponderance cannot come from the thing itself. In what areas of your life are you trying to tip the scale yourself instead of relying on the Agent? How can you surrender that today?
?
Think of a specific worry you are holding right now. According to this lesson, only the distinct Agent can grant existence to what is possible. Are you trusting Him to bring about what you desire, or are you trying to force it? What step can you take to rely on Him more?
