Third Proof: Independence from Causality is Impossible
A conditional proof against independence of preponderance from an agent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical proof that preponderance cannot be independent of an agent because it sometimes depends on one.

Core Idea
If preponderance were independent, it would never depend; but we see it does.

This lesson presents a decisive proof against the idea that preponderance (tarjih) can occur without an agent. The argument is simple but profound: if preponderance were intrinsically independent of an agent, it would never rely on one. Yet our everyday experience shows that things like writing depend on a writer. Therefore, independence is impossible.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to get the full flow of the argument without interruption.
Notice that the author uses a conditional structure.
2
Identify the two parts of the proof
The proof has two main sections: demonstration of the implication (mulazama) and demonstration that preponderance sometimes depends on an agent.
The second part uses an intuitive example.
3
Understand the key principle about essences
The argument hinges on the idea that essences do not change: if something is independent by nature, it cannot become dependent.
This is a fundamental principle in kalam.
4
Consider the intuitive example
Reflect on the example of writing on paper: it clearly depends on a writer. This shows that preponderance (of writing existing over not existing) is not independent.
Similar examples: cutting, breaking, etc.
5
Draw the conclusion
Since the dependence is observed, the premise of independence is false. Therefore, preponderance requires an agent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثانية : لو كان رجحان أحد طرفي الممكن على الآخر غنياً عن المؤثر والمرجح لامتنع توقف هذا الرجحان في موضع من المواضع على حصول المؤثر والمرجح، وهذا الثاني باطل ، فذلك المقدم أيضاً باطل. أما بيان الملازمة فهو أنه لما كان ذلك الرجحان غنياً عن المؤثر امتنع افتقاره إلى المؤثر في شيء من المواضع أصلاً، لأن مقتضيات الحقائق والماهيات لا تتغير البتة ، فإذا كان رجحان أحد طرفي الممكن على الآخر غنياً عن المؤثر من حيث هو هو كان هذا الاستغناء حاصلاً في جميع الصور والغني لذاته عن الشيء يمتنع أن يكون محتاجاً إليه، وأما بيان أن رجحان أحد طرفي الممكن على الآخر قد يتوقف على حصول المؤثر، فلأن العلم البديهي حاصل بأن حصول الكتابة في هذا الكاغد تتوقف على حصول الكاتب، وعلى حصول كل مالا بد منه في كونه كاتباً، وكذا القول في القطع والضرب والكسر وأمثالها من الأفعال
The second proof: If the preponderance of one side of the possible over the other were independent of the agent and the preponderator, then the dependence of this preponderance in any instance on the obtaining of the agent and preponderator would be impossible, and this second is invalid, so that premise is also invalid. As for the demonstration of the implication: it is that when that preponderance is independent of the agent, its need for the agent in any instance is impossible, because the dictates of realities and essences never change. So if the preponderance of one side of the possible over the other is independent of the agent in itself, this independence occurs in all cases, and what is independent by essence of something cannot be in need of it. As for the demonstration that the preponderance of one side of the possible over the other may depend on the obtaining of the agent, it is because intuitive knowledge obtains that the obtaining of writing on this paper depends on the obtaining of the writer, and on obtaining everything necessary for him being a writer, and likewise for cutting, striking, breaking, and similar actions.
Source pages p. 90

Commentary
This passage presents another proof (also labeled 'al-hujja al-thaniya' but distinct in content) against the independence of preponderance from an agent. The proof is a conditional argument: If preponderance were independent (ghani) of an agent, then it would never depend on an agent in any case. The consequent is false because we observe by intuition that preponderance often depends on agents—e.g., writing depends on a writer, cutting on a cutter. Therefore, the antecedent is false. The key step is the principle that essences do not change: if something is independent by its very nature, it remains independent in all instances. Thus, since we see dependence in some instances, independence cannot be the nature of preponderance.

Key Terms
غني
مؤثر
توقف
استغناء
مقتضيات حقائق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that if preponderance is independent in some cases, it could be independent in all, but the argument shows that observed dependence disproves intrinsic independence.
Correction: The argument shows that if preponderance were truly independent by nature, it would never depend in any case. Since we see dependence in some cases, independence cannot be its nature.

Checkpoint
What is the main premise the author refutes in this proof?
A) That preponderance is independent of an agent
B) That preponderance depends on an agent
C) That essences can change
D) That intuitive knowledge is unreliable

Key Takeaway
Preponderance cannot be independent of an agent because we observe it depending on agents in concrete cases like writing.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

When you performed your last deliberate action—writing, speaking, or even making a decision—did you consciously acknowledge that the very preponderance of that action depended entirely on Allah as the Agent? If not, what does that reveal about how you habitually think of your own independence?
?
The proof shows that if preponderance were independent by nature, it could never become dependent. Is there an area of your life—health, wealth, relationships—where you subtly assume independence from Allah? How does this argument strip away that illusion?
?
You have just read that essences are immutable: if something is independent, it remains so. Do you live as though your soul is independent of Allah in its essence? What would change today if you truly internalized that your very existence and every inclination in it are continuously dependent on His agency?

The Locus of Preponderance Must Be External
Author proves that the contingent's preponderance locus is an external cause.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the preponderance of a contingent being cannot be located within the contingent itself, and how this leads to the necessity of a separate cause.

Core Idea
A contingent cannot be the locus of its own preponderance; that would cause circularity.

In this passage, the author concludes a key argument in the proof for a necessary cause. He demonstrates that if the contingent itself were the locus of its preponderance, it would lead to an impossible circularity. Therefore, the locus must be something external, which we call the agent or cause. This argument is central to the Islamic philosophical proof for the existence of a Creator.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage and its literal translation. Try to follow the logical flow: hypothesis, problem, conclusion.
Notice the key term 'دور' (circle/circularity).
2
Understand the argument against self-locus
The author argues: if the locus of preponderance were the possible thing itself, then the preponderance would be prior to itself. Why? Because the locus is prior to the state (preponderance). This creates a circle, which is invalid.
Consider: the possible thing cannot be the subject of its own preponderance because that would require preponderance to precede itself.
3
Grasp the conclusion and definition
The author concludes that the locus must be something external and distinct, which they call 'agent' or 'cause'—an existent whose attribute causes the effect's existence.
This establishes that every contingent needs a separate cause to bring it into existence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
محل هذا الرجحان هو أن تلك الذات حال صيرورتها موجودة : لزم كون هذا الرجحان سابقاً على ذلك الرجحان من حيث أن المحل سابق على الحال . وذلك دور والدور باطل ، فثبت أنه ليس محل هذا الرجحان هو ذلك الممكن الذي هو الأثر ، فلا بد من شيء آخر يغايره ، ويكون محلا لهذا الرجحان ويكون بحيث يلزم من حصول الرجحان فيه حصول الوجود لهذا الأثر ، ولا نريد بالفاعل والمؤثر إلا الموجود الذي من شأنه وصفته ما ذكرناه . فثبت أن الممكن لا يمكن دخوله في الوجود إلا لأجل مؤثر منفصل وذلك هو المطلوب
The locus of this preponderance: if that essence, when it becomes existent, then this preponderance must be prior to that preponderance insofar as the locus is prior to the state. That is a circle, and a circle is invalid. So it is established that the locus of this preponderance is not that possible thing which is the effect; so there must be something else distinct from it, which is a locus for this preponderance, and is such that from the occurrence of preponderance in it the occurrence of existence follows for this effect. And we mean by agent (fāʿil) and cause (muʾaththir) nothing but the existent whose attribute and description are what we have mentioned. So it is established that the possible cannot enter into existence except by reason of a separate cause, and that is what was required.
Source pages p. 90

Commentary
This passage is a conclusion of an argument (likely the end of a proof) establishing that the locus of preponderance for a contingent thing cannot be the contingent thing itself. The author first states a hypothetical: if the locus of preponderance were the same as the essence that becomes existent, then the preponderance would be prior to itself because the locus is prior to the state. That is a circularity (دور), and circularity is false. Therefore, the locus is not the possible thing (the effect). Hence, there must be a separate entity that acts as the locus for the preponderance, and from that preponderance the existence of the effect necessarily follows. The author clarifies that by 'agent' (فاعل) and 'cause' (مؤثر) they mean the existent whose attribute is precisely this. The final conclusion is that a possible thing cannot come into existence except due to a separate cause (مؤثر منفصل). This is the desired conclusion.

Key Terms
رجحان
محل
ممكن
أثر
فاعل
مؤثر
دور

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the locus of preponderance is the efficient cause itself, but here the locus is the subject in which preponderance occurs, not the cause of preponderance.
Correction: The passage distinguishes between the locus (the subject that receives preponderance) and the agent/cause (the existent whose attribute causes the effect). The locus is not the cause; the external entity acts as both locus and cause.

Checkpoint
Why does the author reject the idea that the locus of preponderance is the possible thing itself?
A) Because it leads to an infinite regress
B) Because it leads to circularity
C) Because the possible thing is eternal
D) Because the effect cannot be its own cause

Key Takeaway
The locus of preponderance for a contingent must be an external cause, because self-locus leads to circularity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If every aspect of your existence depends on an external cause, what does that imply about your autonomy and reliance on the Creator? How does this truth confront your sense of self-sufficiency?
?
Consider a habit or trait you thought you could change by sheer willpower (self-caused). In light of the argument that a contingent cannot be its own preponderance locus, how does this reshape your approach to seeking change?
?
The proof shows that a possible thing cannot enter existence except by a separate cause. Have you ever lived as if your actions or existence were uncaused, independent? How does this lesson call you to acknowledge your dependence on Allah in every moment?

The Second Proof: Independence Leads to Contradiction
Conditional proof for necessity of a cause

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical structure of the second proof for the necessity of a cause and why the claim of independence is rationally impossible.

Core Idea
If preponderance were ever independent, it would always be independent—contradicting observable reality.

This passage presents the second proof for the necessity of a cause from classical kalam. It is a conditional argument: if preponderance could be independent (i.e., without a cause), then it would never depend; but we see clear cases like writing that do depend on a cause. Therefore, independence is impossible. The proof relies on the principle that essential attributes are unchanging.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation to grasp the logical flow.
Notice the conditional 'if...then...' structure.
2
Identify the conditional structure
The proof is a conditional argument: antecedent (independence) leads to consequent (permanent independence). The second part shows the consequent is false because we observe dependence. So the antecedent is false.
This is a classic reductio ad absurdum.
3
Understand the implication (mulāzamah)
Why does independence imply permanent independence? Because essential attributes do not change. If preponderance is independent in itself, it must always be independent.
This is the key link in the argument.
4
Examine the refutation
The author cites intuitive knowledge (ʿilm badīhī) that writing depends on a writer. This is a direct counterexample to the claim that preponderance is ever independent.
Observe the use of everyday examples.
5
Conclude the lesson
Since the consequent (permanent independence) is false, the antecedent (independence) is false. Therefore, all preponderance requires a cause.
This establishes the necessity of a cause for all possible things.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثانية : لو كان رجحان أحد طرفي الممكن على الآخر غنياً عن المؤثر والمرجح لامتنع توقف هذا الرجحان في موضع من المواضع على حصول المؤثر والمرجح وهذا الثاني باطل ، فذلك المقدم أيضاً باطل. أما بيان الملازمة فهو أنه لما كان ذلك الرجحان غنياً عن المؤثر امتنع افتقاره إلى المؤثر في شيء من المواضع أصلاً ، لأن مقتضيات الحقائق والماهيات لا تتغير البتة ، فإذا كان رجحان أحد طرفي الممكن على الآخر غنياً عن المؤثر من حيث هو هو كان هذا الاستغناء حاصلاً في جميع الصور والغني لذاته عن الشيء يمتنع أن يكون محتاجاً إليه ، وأما بيان أن رجحان أحد طرفي الممكن على الآخر قد يتوقف على حصول المؤثر ، فلأن العلم البديهي حاصل بأن حصول الكتابة في هذا الكاغد تتوقف على حصول الكاتب ، وعلى حصول كل مالا بد منه في كونه كاتباً ، وكذا القول في القطع والضرب والكسر وأمثالها من الأفعال
The second proof: If the preponderance of one of the two sides of the possible over the other were independent of the cause and the preponderator, then it would be impossible for this preponderance to depend in any situation on the occurrence of the cause and the preponderator. And this second [consequent] is false, so that antecedent is also false. As for the proof of the implication, it is that since that preponderance is independent of the cause, it is impossible for it to be in need of the cause in any situation at all, because the dictates of realities and essences do not change at all. So if the preponderance of one side of the possible over the other is independent of the cause in itself, then this independence occurs in all cases; and that which is inherently independent of something cannot be in need of it. As for the proof that the preponderance of one side of the possible over the other may depend on the occurrence of the cause, it is because intuitive knowledge (al-ʿilm al-badīhī) obtains that the occurrence of writing on this paper depends on the occurrence of the writer and on the occurrence of everything necessary for him to be a writer; and likewise for cutting, striking, breaking, and similar actions.
Source pages p. 90

Commentary
This passage is a complete proof (al-hujjah al-thāniyah) for the necessity of a cause. It is structured as a conditional proof: the antecedent is the claim that the preponderance of one side of a possible over the other is independent of a cause/make-weight. The consequent is that this preponderance would never depend on a cause in any situation. The author argues the consequent is false because we observe that actions like writing do depend on a cause (the writer). Therefore the antecedent (independence) is false. To establish the implication (al-mulāzamah), the author explains that if the preponderance were independent by virtue of its own essence, that independence would be constant across all situations—essential attributes do not change. Hence, it would never need a cause. Then the author points to intuitive knowledge (العلم البديهي) as proof that some preponderances do depend on a cause, such as writing depending on the writer. Thus, the antecedent is false, and preponderance always requires a cause.

الماهيات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Preponderance might be independent in some situations but dependent in others, depending on external factors.
Correction: The argument shows that if preponderance were independent by its own essence, that independence would be unchanging and universal; it cannot be independent in one case and dependent in another. Essential properties do not vary.

Checkpoint
What role does 'intuitive knowledge' (al-ʿilm al-badīhī) play in the second proof?
A) It establishes that essences do not change.
B) It provides a counterexample showing that some preponderances depend on a cause.
C) It proves that all preponderances are independent.
D) It defines what a preponderator is.

Key Takeaway
The second proof demonstrates that preponderance cannot be independent of a cause because if it were, it would never depend, yet we observe clear cases of dependence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If every preponderance requires a cause, then every choice you make—every action, every word—has a cause. What is causing your hesitation right now to truly commit to following the truth you know? Are you willing to trace that hesitation back to its root and confront it before Allah?
?
You just read that if preponderance were independent of a cause, it would never depend. Yet you see your own life full of dependencies—your health depends on food, your knowledge depends on teachers. When you attribute your successes to your own effort alone, are you denying the cause that made that effort possible? Write down one success you had this week and ask: what caused it? Then ask: what caused that cause? Do not stop until you reach the Necessary Being.
?
The proof uses intuitive knowledge (ʿilm badīhī) as evidence—things so obvious you cannot deny them. When you sin, do you not know intuitively that you are responsible? What cause do you blame when you fail? Your excuse is a denial of the preponderator within you. If you truly internalized that every preponderance has a cause, would you ever excuse yourself from accountability again?

Fourth Proof: Eternity of Time Implies a Cause
Complete argument of the fourth proof for a necessary cause from eternity of time.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the argument that even if time is eternal, its contingent parts require a cause, proving the necessity of a creator.

Core Idea
Eternal time still needs a cause because its parts are contingent.

This fourth proof addresses those who assert the eternity of time. The author shows that even on that premise, time's composition of originated parts forces us to posit a cause. It is a careful argument about possibility and causation that ultimately demonstrates the necessity of a creator for all contingent things.

Guided Path
1
Understand the premise: time is eternal
Read the first part of the passage where the author establishes that time has no beginning, using the regress of priorities.
Notice that this is assumed to be accepted by the opponent.
2
Analyze the composition of time
The author then shows that time is composed of successive originated parts, each of which is possible.
3
Examine the causal argument
Consider the two possibilities: time's existence is due to a cause or due to sheer chance. The author rules out chance, concluding a cause is necessary.
4
Generalize the conclusion
The author extends the argument to all possible things, asserting that reason judges them equally in need of a cause.
This connects the proof to the overall goal of proving a creator.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الرابعة : وهي إنما تتم على قول القائلين بقدم المدة، وتقريره أن نقول : القول بقدم المدة لازم، ومتى كان كذلك وجب افتقارها في وجودها إلى المؤثر ، أما بيان أنه لا بد من القول بقدم المدة، فهو أن كل جزء من أجزاء المدة حادث ، وكل حادث فقدمه سابق على وجوده ، وذلك السبق أمر زائد على مجرد العدم لأن العدم المتأخر يشارك العدم المتقدم في مفهوم كونه عدما ، ويخالفه في كونه متقدماً ومتأخراً ، وما به المخالفة غير ما به المشاركة ، فهذا السبق والتقدم أمر زائد على مجرد العدم، فيكون صفة موجودة . ثم تلك القبلية أيضاً فهي مسبوقة بقبلية اخرى ، والكلام فيها كما في الأول . وهذا يوجب أن يكون كل قبل ، مسبوقاً بقبل آخر لا إلى أول . فثبت بهذا الكلام : أن المدة لا أول لها ، ثم نقول : وإنها مركبة من هذه الأجزاء المتعاقبة المتتالية ، وكل واحد منها حادث وكل حادث فهو ممكن ، فالزمان مركب من أجزاء ، وكل واحد منها ممكن لذاته، والمجموع مفتقر إلى تلك الأجزاء ، والمفتقر إلى الممكن أولى بالإمكان ، فمجموع المدة أمر ممكن لذاته . فإما أن يكون رجحان وجوده على عدمه بسبب منفصل ، أو لا بسبب منفصل، بل بمحض الاتفاق فإن كان لمحض الاتفاق من غير سبب أصلا ، فكل ما كان بمحض الاتفاق من غير سبب أصلا لم يمتنع في العقل أن لا يوجد أصلاً ، وعلى هذا التقدير يكون الانقطاع على المدة جائز ، لكنا بينا أن الانقطاع والابتداء عليهما محالان، فوجب أن يقال : إنها واجبة الوجود ، لأجل وجوب سببها ، ولأجل وجوب علتها ، وهذا يدل على أن المدة ممكنة لذاتها ، واجبة لوجوب مؤثرها ، وإذا ثبت أن الأمر كذلك في هذه الصورة وجب أن يكون في سائر الصور كذلك ، ضرورة أن صريح العقل يقضي باستواء كل الممكنات في هذا المعنى والله الهادي إلى الحقائق
The fourth proof: It only succeeds upon the statement of those who affirm the eternity of time (qidam al-muddah). Its presentation is: The statement of the eternity of time is necessary. When that is the case, it must be in need of a cause in its existence. As for the proof that it is necessary to affirm the eternity of time, it is that every part of the parts of time is originated (ḥādith), and every originated thing has its priority (qidam) prior to its existence, and that priority is something additional to mere non-existence, because the later non-existence shares with the earlier non-existence in the concept of being non-existence, and differs from it in being prior and posterior. That by which it differs is other than that by which it shares, so this priority and precedence is something additional to mere non-existence, so it is an existing attribute. Then that priority also is preceded by another priority, and the discussion about it is as in the first. This entails that every priority is preceded by another priority without a first. So by this discourse it is established that time has no beginning. Then we say: It is composed of these successive sequential parts, and each one of them is originated, and every originated thing is possible (mumkin). So time is composed of parts, each of which is possible in itself, and the whole is in need of those parts, and that which is in need of the possible is more deserving of possibility (imkān). So the totality of time is something possible in itself. Then either its preponderance of existence over non-existence is due to a separate cause, or not due to a separate cause, but by sheer chance. If it is by sheer chance without any cause, then everything that is by sheer chance without any cause is not impossible in reason to not exist at all; and on this estimation, cessation (inqiṭāʿ) of time would be permissible. But we have shown that cessation and commencement are both impossible. So it must be said that time is necessary in existence (wājib al-wujūd) due to the necessity of its cause and due to the necessity of its efficient cause. This indicates that time is possible in itself (mumkin li-dhātihā) and necessary due to the necessity of its cause (wājib li-wujūb muʾaththirihā). And when it is established that the case is so in this instance, it must be so in all other instances, necessarily because pure reason judges the equality of all possible things in this respect. And God is the Guide to realities.
Source pages p. 90

Commentary
This passage is a complete proof (al-hujjah al-rābiʿah) that argues from the premise of the eternity of time (admitted by some opponents) to the necessity of a cause. The author breaks it down: First, they establish that time is eternal (no beginning) by noting that every part of time is originated (ḥādith) and each originated thing has a priority (qidam) over its existence, which priority is an attribute distinct from mere non-existence. These priorities form an infinite regress without a first, so time has no beginning. Second, since each part of time is originated and therefore possible (mumkin), and the whole depends on these parts, the whole is also possible. Third, for this possible whole, either its existence is due to a separate cause or due to sheer chance. If by chance, then it would be rationally possible for time not to exist at all, which would imply that cessation of time is possible. But the author claims that cessation and commencement of time are both impossible (according to the proponent of eternity). Therefore, the existence of time must be due to a separate cause, making time possible in itself but necessary through its cause. The author generalizes: since reason judges all possible things equally, all possible things require a cause.

Key Terms
مدة
قدم
حادث
ممكن
قبلية
سبق
عد
اتفاق
مؤثر
علة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If time is eternal, it does not need a cause.
Correction: Even eternal time is composed of contingent (originated) parts, so the whole is contingent and requires a cause for its existence.

Checkpoint
Why does the author argue that time must be caused?
A) Because time is eternal and therefore needs a cause
B) Because each part of time is originated and possible, and the whole is possible, so it requires a cause to exist rather than not
C) Because time has a beginning
D) Because chance cannot produce order

Key Takeaway
Time, though eternal, is composed of contingent parts and thus requires a cause, applying the general principle that all possible things need a cause.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you live as if your existence is contingent on God every moment, or do you feel a sense of self-sufficiency that this proof dismantles? What would it mean for you to truly internalize that you are possible in yourself and necessary only through Him?
?
This argument shows that even an eternal, unending chain of events cannot explain itself—it demands an external cause. What area of your life do you treat as 'self-standing' that you need to re-evaluate as dependent on God?
?
If all possible things are equal in needing a cause, then your very presence here is not a product of chance or your own doing. How does this realization challenge your pride or sense of entitlement? What one change can you make today to acknowledge your dependence on God more concretely?

Time Has No Beginning: The Argument from Priority
Proof that time has no beginning through analysis of priority in non-existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational proof that time has no beginning, based on the analysis of priority in non-existence.

Core Idea
Priority in non-existence is a real attribute, leading to an infinite regress of priorities.

The author begins by analyzing two non-existences: one preceding and one subsequent. They share the concept of non-existence but differ in priority. That difference must be a real attribute, showing that precedence is something extra. Each priority is itself preceded by another, leading to an infinite chain. This proves that time has no beginning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on non-existence priority
Read the first part of the passage carefully, noting how the author distinguishes between preceding and subsequent non-existence.
The key point is that the difference (priority) is a real property, not just a mental concept.
2
Follow the infinite regress argument
Notice that each priority is itself preceded by another priority. This forces an infinite sequence without a first.
3
Connect to the conclusion
The author concludes that time has no beginning because every prior is preceded by another prior ad infinitum.
This establishes the eternity of time a parte ante.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مجرد العدم لأن العدم المتأخر يشارك العدم المتقدم في مفهوم كونه عدما، ويخالفه في كونه متقدما ومتأخرا، وما به المخالفة غير ما به المشاركة، فهذا السبق والتقدم أمر زائد على مجرد العدم، فيكون صفة موجودة. ثم تلك القبلية أيضاً فهي مسبوقة بقبلية أخرى، والكلام فيها كما في الأول. وهذا يوجب أن يكون كل قبل، مسبوقاً بقبل آخر لا إلى أول. فثبت بهذا الكلام: أن المدة لا أول لها، ثم نقول: وإنها مركبة من هذه الأجزاء المتعاقبة المتتالية، وكل واحد منها حادث وكل حادث فهو ممكن، فالزمان مركب من أجزاء، وكل واحد منها ممكن لذاته، والمجموع مفتقر إلى تلك الأجزاء، والمفتقر إلى الممكن أولى بالإمكان، فمجموع المدة أمر ممكن لذاته. فإما أن يكون رجحان وجوده على عدمه بسبب منفصل، أو لا بسبب منفصل، بل بمحض الاتفاق. فإن كان لمحض الاتفاق من غير سبب أصلا، لم يمتنع في العقل، أن لا يوجد أصلا، وعلى هذا التقدير يكون الانقطاع على المدة جائز، لكنا بينا أن الانقطاع والابتداء عليهما محالان، فوجب أن يقال: إنها واجبة الوجود، لأجل وجوب سببها، ولأجل وجوب علتها، وهذا يدل على أن المدة ممكنة لذاتها، واجبة لوجوب مؤثرها، وإذا ثبت أن الأمر كذلك في هذه الصورة وجب أن يكون في سائر الصور كذلك، ضرورة أن صريح العقل يقضي باستواء كل الممكنات في هذا المعنى. والله الهادي إلى الحقائق.
Just non-existence, because the subsequent non-existence shares with the preceding non-existence in the concept of being non-existence, and differs from it in being preceding and subsequent, and that by which they differ is other than that by which they share, so this priority and precedence is an additional thing over mere non-existence, so it is an existing attribute. Then that priorness also is preceded by another priorness, and the discussion about it is as in the first. This necessitates that every prior is preceded by another prior ad infinitum. So it is established by this discussion: that time has no beginning. Then we say: And it is composed of these successive consecutive parts, and each one of them is originated, and every originated thing is possible, so time is composed of parts, each one of them is possible in itself, and the whole is in need of those parts, and that which is in need of the possible is more deserving of possibility, so the totality of time is a thing possible in itself. Then either the preponderance of its existence over its non-existence is due to a separate cause, or not due to a separate cause, but by pure chance. If it is by pure chance without any cause at all, then it is not impossible in the intellect that it would not exist at all, and on this estimation, discontinuity upon time would be permissible, but we have shown that discontinuity and beginning are both impossible for it, so it must be said: It is necessary in existence, due to the necessity of its cause and due to the necessity of its efficient cause, and this indicates that time is possible in itself, necessary due to the necessity of its agent. And when it is established that the matter is so in this case, it must be that it is so in all other cases, due to the necessity that pure reason judges that all possibles are equal in this meaning. And Allah is the Guide to truths.
Source pages p. 91

Commentary
This passage is a proof that time (al-muddah) has no beginning and is composed of parts, each possible, hence the whole is possible and requires a cause. The author begins by analyzing the concept of precedence and subsequence in non-existence: two non-existences share the concept of being non-existent but differ in temporal relation. This difference is an additional attribute (priority), which is real. Since each priority is itself preceded by another priority, an infinite regress of priorities is necessary, implying that time has no beginning. Next, he argues that time is composed of successive originated parts; each part is possible (mumkin) because it is originated. The sum of possibilities is itself possible. Now, the preponderance of its existence over non-existence must be due either to a separate cause or to pure chance. If pure chance were possible, then non-existence of time would be possible, contradicting the proven impossibility of time beginning or ending. Hence, time's existence is necessary through its cause, making it possible in itself but necessary by another. This reasoning applies to all possibles by rational analogy.

Key Terms
العدم المتقدم
العدم المتأخر
السبق
القبلية
المدة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the author, why does the difference between preceding and subsequent non-existence show that priority is real?
A) Because they are two different non-existences.
B) Because they share the concept of non-existence but differ in a relational property.
C) Because non-existence cannot have attributes.
D) Because time is eternal.

Key Takeaway
Time has no beginning because every priority is preceded by another priority ad infinitum.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If every priority is preceded by another priority, then the chain of moments stretches back infinitely. How does this truth affect the way you view the urgency of your repentance? Are you living as if you have infinite time to turn back to Allah, or does this lesson compel you to act now?
?
The argument treats priority as a real attribute, not a mere mental concept. In your own life, do you treat the sequence of your actions as weighty and real, or do you dismiss the past as irrelevant? How does acknowledging the reality of each moment's precedence change your accountability for what you do today?

Time Is Possible and Requires a Cause
Proof that time is possible and requires an external cause.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the proof that time is composed of possible parts and therefore requires a cause for its existence.

Core Idea
Time is a sum of possible parts, so the whole is possible and needs a cause.

Having established that time has no beginning, the author now examines its composition. Time is made of successive originated parts, each possible. The whole is therefore possible. Its existence must be due to a separate cause, not pure chance, because otherwise it could cease to exist, contradicting the impossibility of its beginning or ending.

Guided Path
1
Understand the composition of time
Read the second part of the passage. Note that time is composed of successive parts, each originated and thus possible.
Each part is possible (mumkin) because it is originated (hadith).
2
Evaluate the two possibilities for existence
The author presents two options: existence due to a separate cause or due to pure chance. Reflect on why pure chance would allow non-existence.
3
Reject pure chance and conclude necessity by cause
Since time cannot have a beginning or end (previously proven), pure chance is invalid. Hence time's existence is necessary through its cause.
4
Generalize to all possibles
The author states that this reasoning applies to all possible things by rational analogy.
All possibles are alike in needing a cause for preponderance.

Just non-existence, because the subsequent non-existence shares with the preceding non-existence in the concept of being non-existence, and differs from it in being preceding and subsequent, and that by which they differ is other than that by which they share, so this priority and precedence is an additional thing over mere non-existence, so it is an existing attribute. Then that priorness also is preceded by another priorness, and the discussion about it is as in the first. This necessitates that every prior is preceded by another prior ad infinitum. So it is established by this discussion: that time has no beginning. Then we say: And it is composed of these successive consecutive parts, and each one of them is originated, and every originated thing is possible, so time is composed of parts, each one of them is possible in itself, and the whole is in need of those parts, and that which is in need of the possible is more deserving of possibility, so the totality of time is a thing possible in itself. Source pages p. 91

Commentary
This passage is a proof that time (al-muddah) has no beginning and is composed of parts, each possible, hence the whole is possible and requires a cause. The author begins by analyzing the concept of precedence and subsequence in non-existence: two non-existences share the concept of being non-existent but differ in temporal relation. This difference is an additional attribute (priority), which is real. Since each priority is itself preceded by another priority, an infinite regress of priorities is necessary, implying that time has no beginning. Next, he argues that time is composed of successive originated parts; each part is possible (mumkin) because it is originated. The sum of possibilities is itself possible. Now, the preponderance of its existence over non-existence must be due either to a separate cause or to pure chance. If pure chance were possible, then non-existence of time would be possible, contradicting the proven impossibility of time beginning or ending. Hence, time's existence is necessary through its cause, making it possible in itself but necessary by another. This reasoning applies to all possibles by rational analogy.

Misconception: Time is eternal and uncaused.
Correction: The author shows time is composed of originated parts, so it is possible and requires a cause; its existence is necessary only through its cause.

Checkpoint
Why does the author reject pure chance as the explanation for time's existence?
A) Because pure chance cannot bring anything into existence.
B) Because if it were by chance, time could cease to exist, but it has been proven that time cannot begin or end.
C) Because chance is not a real cause.
D) Because the parts are necessary.

Key Takeaway
Time is possible in itself and requires an external cause for its existence; it is necessary only through its cause.

First Doubt: The Possible Needs a Cause During Persistence
The author states and refutes the first doubt about the persistence of the possible not needing a cause.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first doubt against the need for a cause during persistence and how the author refutes it by showing that preponderance itself is an originated accident requiring a cause.

Core Idea
Persistence is not self-sufficient; even the preponderance during persistence needs a cause.

This lesson addresses a key doubt raised by those who deny that a possible thing requires a cause for its continued existence. The doubt argues that if a cause were needed for origination, it would also be needed for persistence, which is deemed impossible. The author carefully refutes this by analyzing the concept of 'preponderance' (awlawiyyah) and showing that it is an originated attribute that itself demands a cause. Understanding this refutation is crucial for grasping the continuous dependence of all possible beings on their Creator.

Guided Path
1
Read the doubt statement
Start by reading the Arabic passage and its translation to understand the doubt's structure: a conditional argument claiming that need for cause during persistence is false, so original claim is false.
Notice the logical form: If P then Q, not Q, so not P.
2
Parse the conditional explanation
Notice how the author explains that if possibility is the reason for needing a cause, then persistence would also need it, because the thing is still possible during persistence. This sets up the refutation.
3
Identify the objection and refutation
The author anticipates an objection: why not say that during persistence existence becomes preponderant, dispensing with the agent? Then follow the refutation that this preponderance must be either present from origination (impossible) or newly originated.
The refutation uses a dilemma.
4
Trace the chain of dependence
If the preponderance is originated, it needs a separate cause. The persistent thing depends on this preponderance, so it indirectly depends on the separate cause. Thus, persistence requires a cause after all.
The key point: the persistent thing is in need of the separate cause through the chain.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل الرابع في حكاية شبهات القائلين بأن رجحان الممكن لا يتوقف على المرجح. الشبهة الأولى: لو افتقر الممكن إلى المؤثر، لافتقر الباقي حال بقائه إلى المؤثر، وهذا باطل، فذاك باطل. بيان الشرطية أن الممكن ممكن لذاته، فالشيء حال بقائه يجب أن يكون ممكناً، لو كان الإمكان علة للحاجة إلى المؤثر، لزم حصول الحاجة إلى المؤثر حال البقاء، ولا يقال: لم لا يجوز أن يقال إنه حال البقاء صار الوجود به أولى، فلأجل حصول هذه الأولوية يستغنى عن المؤثر؟ لأنا نقول: هذا العذر باطل. وذلك لأن هذه الأولوية الغنية عن المؤثر إما أن يقال: إنها كانت حاصلة حال الحدوث، أو ما كانت حاصلة. فإن كان الأول لزم الاستغناء حال الحدوث، وهو محال. وإن كان الثاني فقد حدثت هذه الأولوية، وهذا الحادث المسمى بالأولوية، هو العلة لوجود الباقي حال بقائه، فيكون الباقي مفتقراً حال بقائه إلى هذا الشيء المسمى بالأولوية، وهذه الأولوية لأجل كونها أمراً حادثاً، تكون مفتقرة إلى السبب المنفصل، والمفتقر إلى المفتقر إلى الشيء يكون مفتقراً إلى ذلك الشيء، فالباقي حال بقائه، يجب أن يكون مفتقراً إلى السبب المنفصل.
Chapter Four concerning the narration of the doubts of those who say that the preponderance of the possible does not depend on the preponderator. The first doubt: If the possible needed the agent, then the persistent (thing) in the state of its persistence would need the agent, and this is false, so that is false. The explanation of the conditional: The possible is possible in itself, so the thing in the state of its persistence must be possible; if possibility were the cause for the need of the agent, then the need for the agent would necessarily occur in the state of persistence. And it cannot be said: Why is it not permissible to say that in the state of persistence existence has become preponderant, so on account of this preponderance it dispenses with the agent? For we say: This excuse is false. That is because this preponderance which dispenses with the agent: either it is said that it was present in the state of origination, or it was not present. If the first, then the dispensation would be necessary in the state of origination, which is impossible. And if the second, then this preponderance has occurred, and this originated thing called preponderance is the cause for the existence of the persistent in the state of its persistence, so the persistent in the state of its persistence is in need of this thing called preponderance, and this preponderance, due to being an originated thing, is in need of the separate cause, and that which is in need of that which is in need of a thing is itself in need of that thing, so the persistent in the state of its persistence must be in need of the separate cause.
Source pages p. 92

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer structure known as a doubt (shubhah). The first doubt argues that if a possible thing needs an agent to bring it into existence, then it would also need that agent for its continued persistence, which is considered absurd; hence the original claim (that the possible needs a cause) is false. The author refutes by dissecting the notion of 'preponderance' (awlawiyyah) during persistence. If the thing becomes preponderant in existence after origination, that preponderance itself is an originated accident. It cannot have existed at origination (otherwise it would dispense with the agent then, impossible), so it must be an originated attribute. This attribute now becomes the cause of the persistent thing's existence, but since it is originated, it in turn requires a separate cause. Thus the persistent thing ultimately depends on that separate cause through a chain. The refutation shows that the need for a cause applies continuously.

المؤثر
الباقي
الإمكان
الأولوية
الحادث
السبب المنفصل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Once something exists, it no longer needs a cause for its continued existence.
Correction: The author shows that the preponderance (awlawiyyah) that makes existence continue is itself an originated accident that requires a cause, so persistence is not self-sufficient.

Checkpoint
In the refutation of the first doubt, what does the author argue about the preponderance (awlawiyyah) during persistence?
A) It is present from origination and therefore dispenses with the cause.
B) It is an originated accident that itself requires a cause.
C) It is a necessary attribute of the persistent thing.
D) It is the same as the preponderance of origination.

Key Takeaway
The need for a cause applies continuously; the preponderance in persistence is an originated accident that itself requires a cause, proving that persistence is not self-sufficient.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You know that everything other than Allah is possible and needs Him at every moment. Is there any area of your life where you act as if you are self-sufficient, independent of Allah, even for a moment? How does this lesson shatter that illusion?
?
The preponderance in persistence is an originated accident—it comes into existence after the thing exists. In your own life, what 'preponderances' or states of being (like health, wealth, relationships) do you assume will persist on their own? How can you remind yourself that even their continuation requires a new act of Allah?
?
If the possibility of a thing is the same at origination and persistence, then the need for a cause is constant. Does your gratitude to Allah for His continuous preservation match the reality that He is sustaining you right now, not just at the moment of your creation? What can you do today to live with that awareness?

The First Doubt: Conditional Argument and Refutation of Appropriateness
Author presents first doubt and refutes 'appropriateness' excuse.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first doubt against the need for a cause for continuance and the refutation of the claim that an 'appropriateness' makes the entity independent.

Core Idea
Possibility requires a cause; even continuance would need one unless an 'appropriateness' appears.

The first doubt argues that if contingents need a cause at origination, they would also need one for continuance—a conclusion that opponents find absurd, so they reject the original premise. The author begins by explaining the conditional and then refutes the objection that a new 'appropriateness' could eliminate the need.

Guided Path
1
Read the doubt and conditional
Carefully read the initial statement of the doubt and the explanation of the conditional linking origination to continuance.
Notice the structure: 'If A then B, but B is false, so A is false.'
2
Understand the role of possibility
The author states that possibility is the cause of need for a cause, so if the contingent persists, it remains possible and thus still needs a cause.
3
Analyze the refutation of appropriateness
The objection suggests that a new 'appropriateness' arises at continuance to eliminate need. The author refutes by examining whether this appropriateness existed at origination or not.
Both cases lead to impossibility or infinite regress.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الشبهة الأولى : لو افتقر الممكن إلى المؤثر ، لافتقر الباقي حال بقائه إلى المؤثر ، وهذا باطل ، فذاك باطل . بيان الشرطية أن الممكن ممكن لذاته فالشيء حال بقائه يجب أن يكون ممكناً ، لو كان الإمكان علة للحاجة إلى المؤثر ، لزم حصول الحاجة إلى المؤثر حال البقاء ، ولا يقال : لم لا يجوز أن يقال إنه حال البقاء صار الوجود به أولى ، فلأجل حصول هذه الأولوية يستغنى عن المؤثر ؟ لأنا نقول : هذا العذر باطل وذلك لأن هذه الأولوية الغنية عن المؤثر إما أن يقال : إنها كانت حاصلة حال الحدوث ، أو ما كانت حاصلة فإن كان الأول لزم الاستغناء حال الحدوث ، وهو محال · وإن كان الثاني فقد حدثت هذه الأولوية ، وهذا الحادث المسمى بالأولوية ، هو العلة لوجود الباقي حال بقائه ، فيكون الباقي مفتقراً حال بقائه إلى هذا الشيء المسمى بالأولوية ، وهذه الأولوية لأجل كونها أمراً حادثاً ، تكون مفتقرة إلى السبب المنفصل، والمفتقر إلى المفتقر إلى الشيء يكون مفتقراً إلى ذلك الشيء، فالباقي حال بقائه، يجب أن يكون مفتقراً إلى السبب المنفصل، فيثبت بما ذكرنا : إنه لو افتقر الممكن إلى المؤثر، لافتقر الباقي حال بقائه إلى المؤثر ، وإنما قلنا : إن الباقي حال بقائه لا يفتقر إلى المؤثر ، لأنا لو فرضنا مؤثراً يؤثر فيه في حال بقائه ، فذلك المؤثر، إما أن يكون له أثر أو لا يكون له أثر ، فإن لم يكن له أثر ، امتنع وصفه بكونه مؤثراً ، وإن كان له أثر فذلك الأثر إما أن يكون شيئاً يصدق عليه ، أنه كان حاصلا قبل هذا التأثير ، أو يصدق عليه أنه ما كان حاصلا قبل هذا التأثير ، فإن كان الأول لزم إيجاد الموجود وهو محال . وإن كان الثاني كان ذلك الأثر أثراً يصدق عليه أنه كان موجوداً قبل ذلك الوقت ، وكل ما كان كذلك فهو حادث ، فهذا المؤثر لا تأثير له إلا في الحادث فلا يكون له أثر في الباقي ، وقد فرضناه مؤثراً في الباقي هذا خلف لا يقال : لم لا يجوز أن يقال إن أثره هو بقاء ذلك الشيء الذي كان موجوداً ؟ لأنا نقول : بقاء ذلك الشيء إما أن يكون نفسه أو غيره ، فإن كان بقاؤه نفسه ، ونفسه كانت حاصلة قبل هذا الوقت ، فحينئذ يكون تأثير هذا المؤثر في تحصيل الحاصل، وهو محال ، وإن كان بقاؤه غيره فحينئذ يكون المسند إلى المؤثر هو هذه الحالة الزائدة الحادثة ، فحينئذ لم يصدر عن المؤثر إلا ما كان حادثاً ، فحينئذ لا يكون لهذا المؤثر تأثيراً في الباقي . وقد فرضناه كذلك . هذا خلف
The first doubt: If the contingent is in need of the efficient cause, then the persistent entity in the state of its persistence would be in need of the efficient cause, and this is false, so that is false. Explanation of the conditional: the contingent is contingent in its essence, so the thing in the state of its persistence must be contingent. If possibility (al-imkan) were the cause of the need for the efficient cause, then the need for the efficient cause would necessarily arise in the state of persistence. And it cannot be said: Why is it not permissible to say that in the state of persistence existence is more appropriate for it, and due to the occurrence of this appropriateness (al-awlawiyya) it becomes independent of the efficient cause? For we say: This excuse is false, because this appropriateness that makes it independent of the efficient cause either is said to have been present in the state of origination or was not present. If the first, then independence would be necessary in the state of origination, which is impossible. And if the second, then this appropriateness has occurred, and this occurring thing named appropriateness is the cause for the existence of the persistent entity in the state of its persistence, so the persistent entity is in need in the state of its persistence of this thing named appropriateness. And this appropriateness, because it is an occurring thing, is in need of an external cause, and that which is in need of that which is in need of something is in need of that something. So the persistent entity in the state of its persistence must be in need of the external cause. So it is established by what we have mentioned: If the contingent were in need of the efficient cause, then the persistent entity in the state of its persistence would be in need of the efficient cause. But we say: The persistent entity in the state of its persistence is not in need of the efficient cause, because if we suppose an efficient cause that produces an effect in it in the state of its persistence, then that efficient cause either has an effect or has no effect. If it has no effect, it is impossible to describe it as an efficient cause. And if it has an effect, then that effect either is something that is true of it that it existed before this causing, or it is true of it that it did not exist before this causing. If the first, then the production of the existent (ijad al-mawjud) would be necessary, which is impossible. And if the second, then that effect is an effect that is true of it that it did not exist before that time, and everything that is like that is occurring (hadith). So this efficient cause has effect only in the occurring, so it has no effect in the persistent entity, and we supposed it to be an efficient cause in the persistent entity; this is a contradiction. It cannot be said: Why is it not permissible to say that its effect is the continuance (baqa') of that thing which existed? For we say: The continuance of that thing is either its essence or something else. If its continuance is its essence, and its essence existed before this time, then the effect of this efficient cause would be in attaining what is already attained, which is impossible. And if its continuance is something else, then the attribution to the efficient cause is this supervening occurring state, and then nothing issues from the efficient cause except what is occurring, and then this efficient cause has no effect in the persistent entity. And we supposed it to be so; this is a contradiction.
Source pages p. 92

Commentary
This passage is the presentation and refutation of the first doubt (shubha) raised by those who argue that if a contingent being needs an efficient cause for its initial existence, it also needs that cause for its continuance. The author first states the conditional argument: If the contingent needs a cause at origination, it would also need it at continuance, but the latter is false, so the former is false. He then explains the conditional by analyzing possibility (imkan) as the cause of need. He anticipates an objection: perhaps continuance brings a new ‘appropriateness’ (awlawiyya) that makes the being independent. The author refutes this by examining whether this appropriateness existed at origination or not. If it did, independence would hold at origination (impossible); if it is new, then it itself is an occurring thing needing a cause, leading to an infinite regress. Next, he directly proves that the persistent entity does not need a cause by considering the hypothetical efficient cause at continuance. He argues that such a cause either has no effect (contradicting its definition) or its effect is either pre-existing (impossible: producing the already existing) or new (making the cause only effective in the new, not the persistent). He covers a further objection that the effect might be continuance itself, and argues that continuance is either identical to the essence (leading to producing the already existing) or a supervening state (again only affecting the new). Thus the doubt is refuted.

ممكن
مؤثر
إمكان
أولوية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: A new 'appropriateness' can make a persistent entity independent of a cause without itself being caused.
Correction: The author shows that if the appropriateness is new, it itself is an event needing a cause, leading to a regress.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the consequence if the 'appropriateness' that makes the entity independent existed already at origination?
A) The entity would not need a cause at continuance.
B) The entity would not need a cause at origination.
C) The appropriateness would need its own cause.
D) The entity would need two causes.

Key Takeaway
The first doubt is refuted by showing that the 'appropriateness' either already existed (making origination independent) or is a new event requiring its own cause.

?
The lesson shows that any 'appropriateness' that arises must itself have a cause. How does this challenge the way you attribute changes in your life to your own efforts rather than to Allah's continuous act of creation and sustenance? What would change in your daily attitude if you truly internalized that you need Allah for every moment of your continued existence?

Direct Proof that Continuance Needs No Cause
Author directly argues that persistent entity needs no cause for continuance.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the direct argument that a persistent entity does not require an efficient cause for its continuance.

Core Idea
A cause for continuance would either have no effect or produce the already existing—both impossible.

After refuting the appeal to appropriateness, the author provides a more direct proof: if we suppose an efficient cause acting at continuance, its effect must be either pre-existing or new—both lead to contradictions. This lesson examines that argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the direct argument
Read the passage starting from 'وإنما قلنا' which presents the direct refutation.
The author now assumes a hypothetical cause for continuance.
2
Analyze the two possibilities for the effect
The effect of that cause is either already existing (impossible: producing the existent) or new (then the cause only affects the new, not the persistent entity).
3
Consider the objection about continuance itself
An opponent might say the effect is continuance (baqa'). The author responds that continuance is either the same as the essence (leading to producing what already exists) or a supervening state (then it is new, and the cause affects only the new, not the persistent entity).
This reinforces the impossibility.

له أثر فذلك الأثر إما أن يكون شيئاً يصدق عليه ، أنه كان حاصلا قبل هذا التأثير ، أو يصدق عليه أنه ما كان حاصلا قبل هذا التأثير ، فإن كان الأول لزم إيجاد الموجود وهو محال . وإن كان الثاني كان ذلك الأثر أثراً يصدق عليه أنه كان موجوداً قبل ذلك الوقت ، وكل ما كان كذلك فهو حادث ، فهذا المؤثر لا تأثير له إلا في الحادث فلا يكون له أثر في الباقي ، وقد فرضناه مؤثراً في الباقي هذا خلف لا يقال : لم لا يجوز أن يقال إن أثره هو بقاء ذلك الشيء الذي كان موجوداً ؟ لأنا نقول : بقاء ذلك الشيء إما أن يكون نفسه أو غيره ، فإن كان بقاؤه نفسه ، ونفسه كانت حاصلة قبل هذا الوقت ، فحينئذ يكون تأثير هذا المؤثر في تحصيل الحاصل، وهو محال ، وإن كان بقاؤه غيره فحينئذ يكون المسند إلى المؤثر هو هذه الحالة الزائدة الحادثة ، فحينئذ لم يصدر عن المؤثر إلا ما كان حادثاً ، فحينئذ لا يكون لهذا المؤثر تأثيراً في الباقي . وقد فرضناه كذلك . هذا خلف
The first doubt: If the contingent is in need of the efficient cause, then the persistent entity in the state of its persistence would be in need of the efficient cause, and this is false, so that is false. Explanation of the conditional: the contingent is contingent in its essence, so the thing in the state of its persistence must be contingent. If possibility (al-imkan) were the cause of the need for the efficient cause, then the need for the efficient cause would necessarily arise in the state of persistence. And it cannot be said: Why is it not permissible to say that in the state of persistence existence is more appropriate for it, and due to the occurrence of this appropriateness (al-awlawiyya) it becomes independent of the efficient cause? For we say: This excuse is false, because this appropriateness that makes it independent of the efficient cause either is said to have been present in the state of origination or was not present. If the first, then independence would be necessary in the state of origination, which is impossible. And if the second, then this appropriateness has occurred, and this occurring thing named appropriateness is the cause for the existence of the persistent entity in the state of its persistence, so the persistent entity is in need in the state of its persistence of this thing named appropriateness. And this appropriateness, because it is an occurring thing, is in need of an external cause, and that which is in need of that which is in need of something is in need of that something. So the persistent entity in the state of its persistence must be in need of the external cause. So it is established by what we have mentioned: If the contingent were in need of the efficient cause, then the persistent entity in the state of its persistence would be in need of the efficient cause. But we say: The persistent entity in the state of its persistence is not in need of the efficient cause, because if we suppose an efficient cause that produces an effect in it in the state of its persistence, then that efficient cause either has an effect or has no effect. If it has no effect, it is impossible to describe it as an efficient cause. And if it has an effect, then that effect either is something that is true of it that it existed before this causing, or it is true of it that it did not exist before this causing. If the first, then the production of the existent (ijad al-mawjud) would be necessary, which is impossible. And if the second, then that effect is an effect that is true of it that it did not exist before that time, and everything that is like that is occurring (hadith). So this efficient cause has effect only in the occurring, so it has no effect in the persistent entity, and we supposed it to be an efficient cause in the persistent entity; this is a contradiction. It cannot be said: Why is it not permissible to say that its effect is the continuance (baqa') of that thing which existed? For we say: The continuance of that thing is either its essence or something else. If its continuance is its essence, and its essence existed before this time, then the effect of this efficient cause would be in attaining what is already attained, which is impossible. And if its continuance is something else, then the attribution to the efficient cause is this supervening occurring state, and then nothing issues from the efficient cause except what is occurring, and then this efficient cause has no effect in the persistent entity. And we supposed it to be so; this is a contradiction.
Source pages p. 92

Commentary
This passage is the presentation and refutation of the first doubt (shubha) raised by those who argue that if a contingent being needs an efficient cause for its initial existence, it also needs that cause for its continuance. The author first states the conditional argument: If the contingent needs a cause at origination, it would also need it at continuance, but the latter is false, so the former is false. He then explains the conditional by analyzing possibility (imkan) as the cause of need. He anticipates an objection: perhaps continuance brings a new ‘appropriateness’ (awlawiyya) that makes the being independent. The author refutes this by examining whether this appropriateness existed at origination or not. If it did, independence would hold at origination (impossible); if it is new, then it itself is an occurring thing needing a cause, leading to an infinite regress. Next, he directly proves that the persistent entity does not need a cause by considering the hypothetical efficient cause at continuance. He argues that such a cause either has no effect (contradicting its definition) or its effect is either pre-existing (impossible: producing the already existing) or new (making the cause only effective in the new, not the persistent). He covers a further objection that the effect might be continuance itself, and argues that continuance is either identical to the essence (leading to producing the already existing) or a supervening state (again only affecting the new). Thus the doubt is refuted.

Key Terms
باقٍ
إيجاد الموجود
تحصيل الحاصل
حادث

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Continuance is a separate entity that requires a new efficient cause to be sustained.
Correction: The author argues that continuance is either identical to the essence or a supervening state; in both cases, positing a cause leads to impossibility.

Checkpoint
According to the direct argument, what is the consequence if the supposed efficient cause produces an effect that existed before the causing?
A) The effect is new and the cause is effective.
B) The cause would be producing what already exists, which is impossible.
C) The cause would have no effect.
D) The cause would be unnecessary.

Key Takeaway
A cause for continuance is impossible because it would either produce nothing new or reproduce what already exists.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have just read that your own continued existence cannot be caused by any secondary means—not health, not wealth, not relationships. How does that confront your daily reliance on these things? Before you move on, write down one area where you have placed your trust in a created cause for your stability, and commit to turning to Allah alone for that very thing.
?
If no cause can preserve you except the One who first gave you life, then every moment you are kept from annihilation is a direct act of Al-Bari. Does this thought make you feel more aware of His nearness, or do you find it unsettling? Why? Choose one action today that will remind you of this dependence, such as a specific dhikr or a pause before an action.
?
The author concludes that a cause for continuance is impossible. Reflect on a time you feared losing something you thought you had 'secured'—a job, a skill, a relationship. In light of this lesson, what was the real source of its continuance? Will you allow this to reshape how you plan and worry about tomorrow?

The Second Doubt: The Efficient Cause Cannot Act on Existent or Non-Existent
Presentation and refutation of the second doubt about efficient causality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the structure of the second doubt against efficient causality and why its two alternatives are considered impossible by the objectors.

Core Idea
The doubt claims causality is impossible because the cause can act neither on an existing nor on a non-existing effect.

This lesson presents a classic skeptical argument against causality. The objector argues that an efficient cause's effecting must occur either when the effect already exists or when it does not exist, and both are impossible. Understanding this doubt is essential before we can see how it is refuted.

Guided Path
1
Introduce the doubt structure
The doubt presents a dichotomy: either the cause acts on an existing effect or on a non-existing effect. Both are claimed to be impossible, so causality is false.
This is the objector's argument, not the author's own view.
2
Examine the first alternative
The cause acting on an existing effect is impossible because that would be producing what already exists (ijad al-mawjud) and attaining what is already attained (tahsil al-hasil).
3
Examine the second alternative
The cause acting on a non-existing effect is impossible because the effect remains in pure non-existence, no change occurs, so no effect issues from the cause.
This leaves the cause with no effect at all.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الشبهة الثانية : إن تأثير المؤثر في الأثر إما أن يكون حال وجود الأثر ، أو حال عدمه والقسمان باطلان ، فكان القول بالتأثير باطلاً ، وإنما قلنا : إنه يمتنع أن يؤثر المؤثر في الأثر حال وجود الأثر : لامتناع إيجاد الموجود ، وتحصيل الحاصل. وإنما قلنا : إنه يمتنع أن يؤثر في الأثر حال عدم الأثر لأن الأثر حال عدمه باقي كما كان ، وإذا بقي الأثر كما كان على العدم المحض ، والنفي الصرف ، فحينئذ لم يصدر عن هذا الشيء المسمى بالمؤثر ، أثر في هذا الوقت البتة
The second doubt: The effect of the efficient cause on the effect is either in the state of the effect's existence, or in the state of its non-existence. And both divisions are false, so the claim of effecting (al-ta'thir) is false. And we say: It is impossible for the efficient cause to affect the effect in the state of the effect's existence, because of the impossibility of producing the existent (ijad al-mawjud) and attaining what is already attained (tahsil al-hasil). And we say: It is impossible for it to affect the effect in the state of the effect's non-existence, because the effect in its state of non-existence remains as it was, and if the effect remains as it was in pure non-existence and absolute negation, then nothing issues from this thing called the efficient cause as an effect at this time at all.
Source pages p. 93

Commentary
This passage presents and refutes the second doubt. The doubt states that the efficient cause's effecting must occur either while the effect exists or while it does not exist. Both options are argued to be impossible. First, effecting an already existing thing is impossible because it would be producing what already exists (ijad al-mawjud) or attaining what is already attained, both of which are absurd. Second, effecting a non-existent thing is impossible because the effect remains in pure non-existence, so no change occurs; the cause has no effect at all. Therefore, the very notion of an efficient cause effecting anything is false. This argument is used by the objectors to deny efficient causality entirely, but the author is presenting it as a doubt to be refuted (likely in further text not included here). The passage shows a classic skeptical argument about causation.

Key Terms
تأثير
مؤثر
أثر
وجود
عدم
إيجاد الموجود
تحصيل الحاصل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That this doubt successfully disproves causality and that the author endorses this argument.
Correction: The author is presenting this as a doubt to be refuted, not as his own position. The refutation (elsewhere) shows that the cause acts by bringing the effect from non-existence to existence at the moment of origination.

Checkpoint
According to the second doubt, why is it impossible for an efficient cause to affect an effect in the state of the effect's existence?
A) Because the effect would be destroyed.
B) Because it would be producing what already exists, which is impossible.
C) Because the cause would then be unnecessary.
D) Because the effect would change its essence.

Key Takeaway
The second doubt against causality argues that a cause cannot act on an existent or non-existent effect, thus denying efficient causality altogether.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever felt that your efforts are meaningless because the outcome already seems determined? This doubt superficially argues that causes can act neither on what exists nor on what does not exist. In your own life, where do you need to remember that Allah alone creates true change, and your actions are only means? Write down one situation and commit to making du'a recognizing Allah as the real cause.
?
When you examine your heart, do you find any subtle belief that you can 'produce' results by your own power, or that certain things are 'impossible' for Allah? The doubt's error is in assuming a false dilemma. How can you guard against such a mindset by reflecting on Allah's creative power? Name one personal habit you will adopt this week to increase your reliance on Him.
?
If you truly believed that your actions have no real effect—as the doubt implies—how would your trust in Allah's promises be affected? In what area of your obedience to Allah do you need to renew your certainty that He is the One Who brings about results, and that your acts of worship are not in vain? What step will you take today to demonstrate that certainty?
